If one enters the mountains and forests and encounters venomous or ferocious beasts, merely focusing the mind on a single thought of the true scripture will ensure the mountain spirits guard and protect them, causing the fierce beasts to retreat without harm. If one enters rivers or seas to gather treasures or seek precious things and encounters violent winds that threaten to capsize their vessel, reciting this true scripture according to its prescribed method will instantly still the winds and waves, allowing safe passage to shore. The benefits of the true scripture extend beyond averting heavenly disasters, ensuring bountiful harvests, and bringing peace to the common folk. Even sudden calamities—perils of mountains, waters, winds, and waves—can be rendered harmless.

If one enters the mountains and forests and encounters venomous or ferocious beasts, merely focusing the mind on a single thought of the true scripture, never forgetting this precious and wondrous dharma, will ensure the mountain spirits guard and protect them, causing the fierce beasts to retreat naturally without harming the scripture-holder. If one enters rivers or seas to gather treasures or seek precious objects and encounters violent winds that drift their vessel, reciting this true scripture according to the method prescribed in the scripture will cause the gale to subside, the waves to still, and the vessel to reach shore safely, with no harm befalling the scripture-holder.

Mountains and forests are perilous places, where fierce beasts and venomous creatures cannot be approached. Upon encountering them, one is surely harmed. Yet merely focusing the mind on a single thought of the true scripture—briefly uniting with the Dao—causes the beasts to retreat. This is because all living beings originate from the same primordial *qi*, differing only in purity and turbidity. In the beginning, Heaven and Earth were undivided, and humans and creatures coexisted in harmony, with venomous beasts and ferocious creatures posing no threat. Later, human hearts grew cunning, and they began to harm creatures, provoking creatures to harm them in return. If one consistently does good deeds, maintains a virtuous heart, reveres and believes in the true scripture, and never forgets it, they are a great person of virtue. Entering mountains and forests, they will not encounter venomous creatures or fierce beasts. If an ordinary person without merit enters the mountains and happens upon such creatures, not clinging to life in fear of death, remaining unflustered and unwavering, steadfastly believing in the true dharma, focusing single-mindedly, and silently reciting a thought of the true scripture, their heart will unite with the Dao. The mountain spirits will guard and protect them, and the venomous creatures and fierce beasts will naturally retreat.

Creatures are dull beings within the Dao. Though their natures may be venomous and harmful, each has what they revere, fear, and avoid. Thus, birds of prey honor the phoenix, fierce beasts pay homage to the *lin*, and venomous creatures like snakes, scorpions, centipedes, and millipedes fear storks, cranes, and herons, vanishing without deploying their venom. The immortals and perfected ones understand the mechanisms of life and transformation, the origins of control and restraint. A single incantation or talismanic water can subdue venomous snakes, resolve the perils of tigers, reverse winds, and turn back the heavens. How much more so the *Huangjing*, which is the source of all dharmas and the ancestor of Heaven and Earth? The life and death of all things depend on Heaven and Earth, rooted in the Great Dao. Focusing the mind on a single thought of this scripture, one need not fear creatures. The radiance of the dharma-treasure and the protection of the deities ensure that, though creatures may be dull, their nature reveres the Dao, fears Heaven, and stands in awe of the divine, causing them to retreat naturally.

Rivers, lakes, seas, and oceans are perilous places of wind and waves, where Heaven’s wrath seems inescapable. Yet this scripture is the king of all dharmas, and wind, thunder, hail, and lightning are the wondrous manifestations of Heaven’s divine power. Reciting this true scripture according to its method unites one with Heaven and the Dao, causing Heaven to soften its might, the winds to subside, and the waves to still, allowing the vessel to reach shore safely.

**Verification:**
The *Book of Changes* states: "Treading upon a tiger’s tail, it bites the man—misfortune." Confucius said: "Facing a tiger bare-handed or fording a river—dying without regret." Yet this scripture says that focusing the mind on a single thought or reciting it according to its method causes creatures to retreat and winds and waves to still. The *Daodejing* states: "Those who align with the Dao are not seized by birds of prey, stung by venomous creatures, or gored by tigers or rhinoceroses. Their claws and fangs remain sheathed, and they dare not harm." Many doubt that Confucianism and Daoism contradict each other, but consider: When Yu crossed the Yangtze, a yellow dragon lifted its head and departed. When Liu Kun governed Jiangling, he reversed the wind to extinguish a fire, and when he governed Hongnong, tigers crossed the river to the north. When Master Yi crossed the river, his reverence stilled the winds. Thus, the *Book of Changes* and Confucius refer to those who act recklessly without the Dao, while the *Huangjing* and *Daodejing* speak of those who honor the scriptures and align with the Dao.

The scripture’s merit can even deliver one from the brute realms and sever karmic poisons—how could those who uphold it walk unscathed among venomous creatures? *Chapter on Merits and Virtues, Continued (Chapter Twenty)*

When in battle, with polearms locked and blades crossed, silently recite this true scripture with focused mind, and all hostile forces will scatter and disperse. Does the true scripture’s power merely resolve the perils of beasts and storms?

When in battle, with weapons raised and polearms engaged, blades clashing between armies, silently recite this true scripture with focused mind, and all hostile forces assaulting you on the field will scatter and disperse.

**Textual Verification:**
Weapons are instruments of misfortune; halberds, matters of peril. In the heat of charge and defense, arrows and stones flying, blades locked in combat like tigers in mortal struggle—no force can break the deadlock. Yet silently reciting this true scripture, aligning the heart with the Dao, how could hostile forces not retreat?

The art of war states: "One can break the morale of an entire army; one can shake the resolve of its general." Silent recitation of the true scripture seizes their spirits, strips their courage, and through the scripture’s divine efficacy, the enemy loses heart, their will to kill and fight dissolved. Turning from evil to virtue, they retreat and disperse of their own accord.

"Army" here does not refer to the fixed number of twelve thousand five hundred, nor does "formation" imply a set battle array. It simply denotes the moment when war is joined and ranks are drawn, the clash of attack. The repetition of "polearms engaged" and "blades crossed" is not redundant; it merely follows the scripture’s phrasing of "weapons and blades clashing." To silently recite the true scripture is not to seek mere escape from slaughter, but to devote oneself wholeheartedly, even unto death, without straying from the Dao.

"All" means the multitude; "hostile forces" refers not to "evil people" and "bandit factions" as separate categories, but simply to rebellious enemies. "Scatter and disperse" means to retreat and turn back, to disperse and return to their own.

**Resolving Doubts:**
When Lord Lao transformed the Hu barbarians, the kingdom of Yutian besieged him. When the Heavenly Emperor resided in his celestial palace, the demon kings raised armies to contend with him. When the Buddha was in Kapilavastu, the six kings—Ajātaśatru and others—rebelled. When Confucius taught the world, he was barred from gathering firewood in Chen and erased from records in Song. All were great sages, embodiments of the Dao, manifestations of the Dharma-body, constantly reciting the true scripture. Yet they faced those who sought to harm them.

Later, Lord Lao radiated the light of the Dao, and Yutian submitted. The Emperor manifested celestial radiance, and the demon kings withdrew. The Buddha emanated the light of the Dharma, and the six kings returned to allegiance. Confucius shone with the light of virtue, and when he plucked his zither and sang thrice, the men of Kuang laid down their arms. Thus, the enemies retreated of their own accord.

Consider how many bandits have sworn not to harm the virtuous, and how many good people have faced peril yet survived. Some doubt the efficacy of scriptural merit, citing Emperor Huizong and Emperor Qinzong of Song, who honored the Dao yet suffered humiliation in the northern lands, or Emperor Wu of Liang, who revered the Buddha yet starved to death in Taicheng. Yet these three rulers’ devotion to Dao and Buddha was but a hollow reputation, not true commitment.

While the passions and desires remain unsevered, one cannot govern the world through the Dao, hence these calamities arise. If one truly embodies the Dao, steadfastly believing in the power of the scriptures, even a common man or woman may avoid disaster. To doubt the integrity of those who die for righteousness or disbelieve the words of the scriptures is indeed mistaken.

Alas! The praise of unblemished integrity serves to exhort the world; the recounting of scriptural merit serves to inspire people toward goodness. In essence, the scriptures’ purport does not transcend the Dao of one’s own nature and being. When the six thieves of the senses rebel, focusing the mind single-heartedly on the Dao causes the six thieves to withdraw. Thus, when encountering warfare, Heaven resolves the peril. As Lord Lao said: *"Armor and weapons cannot harm."* And Guanyin declared: *"Blades shatter into fragments of their own accord."* Such is the inevitable principle. How could these words deceive us?

**Chapter Twenty-One (Continued) of the Meritorious Virtues Section**
If one finds oneself in prison or bound by fetters, purify the mind, settle the thoughts, and meditate upon this scripture—wrongful accusations will be redressed, and liberation will be attained. The benefits of this scripture extend far beyond merely causing bandits and soldiers to retreat of their own accord.

If one has inadvertently committed an offense and is imprisoned or bound in fetters, purify the heart and mind, calm the thoughts, and meditate upon this true scripture—wrongful accusations will naturally be clarified, and liberation will be attained.

**Textual Verification:**
Prisons are meant to confine the guilty. The principle that virtue brings fortune and vice brings misfortune is the constant Way of Heaven. Once imprisoned for a crime, one cannot be immediately released—how then can it be said that meditating upon this scripture brings liberation? The phrase *"wrongful accusations will be redressed"* implies that the person in question must have been inherently good in daily life. Perhaps they bore karmic debts from past lives, or their fate in this life led to misfortune; perhaps they were wrongly implicated by others or ensnared while attempting to rescue someone; or perhaps they were falsely accused due to the misdeeds of the deceased. Once imprisoned, all these cases are wrongful. Without resentment toward Heaven or blame toward others, enduring hardship with equanimity, free from distracting thoughts—this is what it means to purify the mind, settle the thoughts, and meditate upon the true scripture. Heaven aids the fortunate, and wrongful oppression will naturally be cleared without the need for argument. No prolonged struggle is necessary, for there is no principle in the world by which the innocent remain forever oppressed. Liberation comes swiftly.

*"Prison"* (牢) refers to the confinement of the guilty; *"custody"* (狱) refers to awaiting trial. Today’s prisons, jails, and detention centers vary in severity, but all are places where the guilty are held. The two terms are not fundamentally distinct. Fetters and shackles restrain the guilty—those who suffer them are deemed guilty, for such is the nature of misfortune.

Some say: *"The character for 'prison' (牢) contains 'ox,' and 'custody' (狱) contains 'dog.' If one does not consume oxen or dogs, one will forever avoid prison."* This is merely to teach people not to kill living beings, not to indulge their appetites, and not to invite disaster through craving. Such is an ancillary meaning of the scripture, appended here for reference.

The scripture’s benefit lies in redressing wrongs. Even if one has committed offenses in the past, sincere repentance and renewal, free from resentment, coupled with the scripture’s salvific power and unwavering devotion, will eventually lead to liberation.

King Wen, who attained the Dao and became a sage, was imprisoned in Youli yet composed the *Book of Changes* and was pardoned. Gongye Chang, who aspired to the Dao and became a worthy, was bound in fetters yet soon released. Xu Shengong and Zhang Ziyang, who attained the Dao and became immortals, endured imprisonment in Changyi and the torments of the Three Trials—yet through their steadfast cultivation of the supreme Dao and their wondrous transmission of the *Awakening to Reality*, they both achieved liberation.

Not only sages and worthies—even the foolish, if they cast aside deluded thoughts and steadfastly believe in the true scripture, will naturally escape the perils of prison.

**Resolving Doubts:**
In the most ancient times, there were no criminals and no penal systems. The Yellow Emperor captured Chi You, devised shackles, and drew boundaries to enforce prohibitions—thus the concept of penal systems arose. After the Three Dynasties, the penal system was fully established.

The scriptures existed before the kalpas, first expounded by the Primordial Beginning in the heavens. How then can they speak of prisons? The reason is that in every kalpa and every place, there are both good and evil; evil naturally incurs retribution, and the principle of imprisonment is inherent. The scriptures encompass all methods and eternally benefit all beings, containing every principle and foretelling the efficacy of deliverance from sin.

The Buddhist scriptures say: *"By the power of Guanyin, fetters and shackles soon fall away."* The Confucian classics say: *"The noble person is mindful of punishment."* The *Book of Changes* speaks of *"misfortunes that are not one’s fault."* Have they not also spoken of prisons? Thus, we know that the scriptures are not the words of recent times but primordial and mysterious teachings.

In essence, a single thought aligned with principle becomes a hall of fortune; a single selfish thought becomes a prison of karma. If desires and delusions are not eradicated, the body becomes a pit of karma, and prison is already present. Mencius said: *"The difference between humans and beasts is slight."* The character for *"prison"* (牢) contains *"ox,"* and *"custody"* (狱) contains *"dog."* The creators of these characters had profound intent. For people of the present and future ages, steadfastly believing in this scripture will ensure that in every kalpa and every place, prison is avoided.

**Chapter Twenty-Two (Continued) of the Meritorious Virtues Section**
If one is afflicted by malevolent spirits, demonic thieves, or manifold sufferings, reciting this true scripture in accordance with its methods will cause all evil beings to flee far away, and one will naturally be at peace.

The divine efficacy of the Jade Scripture—how could it be limited to merely resolving the perils of prison?

If one is afflicted by any manner of evil demons, spirits, ghosts, thieves, or manifold sufferings, reciting this true scripture in accordance with its methods will cause all malevolent spirits, ghosts, and thieves to flee far away, and one will naturally be at peace and free from harm.

**Textual Verification:**
*"If one is afflicted"* (若为) is equivalent to *"if one suffers."* *"Malevolent spirits"* (邪精) refers to all that is unorthodox; *"ghosts"* (鬼) are the numinous beings of the yin realm; *"thieves"* (贼) are those who harm people and things. Some say that *"thieves"* here refers to evil people. The previous section already mentioned that *"bandits and soldiers all retreat of their own accord."* Why then is *"thieves"* here interpreted as referring to people?

Generally, evil demons, spirits, and ghosts lie in wait for opportunities and exploit vulnerabilities, entering through a person’s unorthodox thoughts. Their coming is untraceable, and their going is unseen. They inflict harm in secret, making their actions unpredictable—hence they are called *"ghostly thieves."*

From celestial demons above to earthly sprites below—mountain specters, water anomalies, tree spirits, metal essences, fire imps, plant oddities, the essences of birds and beasts, ice and charcoal apparitions, *chī* monstrosities, ghostly spirits—all manner of unorthodox entities abound. These bring myriad afflictions: hurling bricks and tiles, startling chickens and dogs to demand sacrifices, disturbing households. Some cause people to become disoriented, their spirits wandering in confusion, raving without reason, dashing about wailing, lost in stupor, leaping and retching, howling in depravity, drunk in folly, harmed and ruined, unable to rest or eat, day and night in terror. In life, they know no peace; in death, they remain bound and obstructed—such are the myriad afflictions. *"Add"* (加) means calamity befalling a person.

If one recites this true scripture in accordance with the proper method, how could one be afflicted by unorthodox essences, ghostly thieves, and the myriad afflictions? Here, *"in accordance with the proper method"* does not mean mere ease of practice but rather single-minded devotion, aligning with the true Dao. When reciting this scripture, one need only follow the prescribed method of chanting, bowing in reverence, and recitation—then all unorthodox essences and ghostly thieves will flee far away.

Another interpretation holds that reciting this true scripture in accordance with the proper method allows a Daoist practitioner to recite on behalf of the afflicted, driving away all unorthodox entities and ensuring natural peace. No talismanic waters, exorcisms, medicinal remedies, or incantations are needed—merely hearing the scripture, the afflicted will naturally find peace, free from the myriad afflictions.

It is said that the Ancestral Celestial Master subdued all demons with his methods. The Dark Emperor cultivated the true Dao, vanquishing all unorthodox essences and governing humans and ghosts. The Perfected Sage Sa overcame demonic forces in his propagation of the Dao. Lord Lao subdued celestial demons. The Buddha’s mantras tamed demons. The former Confucian scholar Fan Zhongyan, having lodged on a battlefield, heard only the cries of small chickens, and ghosts ceased their mischief. Master Cheng subdued the Great-Headed Ghost, and when a multitude of ghosts moved his bed, his mind remained unmoved—all ghosts then retreated into hiding. Thus it is said: *"When the Dao is lofty, dragons and tigers submit; when virtue is weighty, ghosts and spirits revere."*

Reciting this true scripture in accordance with the proper method—this secret and sacred canon, the most holy of true words—unlocks the mechanism unseen by ghosts and spirits. How could unorthodox essences and ghostly thieves fail to flee far away?

**Resolving Doubts:**
In essence, the Three Corpses within the human body, the Five Orifices, the Seven *Po* souls, the Seven Emotions, the Six Desires, scattered thoughts, and deluded imaginings—all these yin tendencies are unorthodox essences and ghostly thieves. If one awakens to emptiness and clarity, wielding the sword of wisdom to slay demons, and extinguishes desires, then unorthodox essences and ghostly thieves will flee far away.

**Chapter Twenty-Three of the Meritorious Virtues Section**
If one seeks descendants and, in accordance with the proper method, venerates and recites this scripture, the Emperor commands the celestial bureaus to examine the cinnabar registers. Among the nine ranks, the Four Fruits of Immortals, whose karmic cycles align with the destined time, will be dispatched to descend and be born as one’s child—gifted with exceptional talent and eloquence, esteemed among humanity.

Does the merit of this scripture merely subdue unorthodox entities? If one, childless and suffering, seeks descendants and recites this scripture in accordance with the method of venerating the canon, the Supreme Emperor will command the celestial bureaus to thoroughly examine the cinnabar records of immortals. Among the nine ranks, the Four Fruits of Immortals, whose cycles and numbers align with the destined time, will descend and be born as one’s child. To those without descendants who recite the scripture, such a child will be born—brilliant in talent, skilled in debate, wise and discerning, attaining the noble station of humanity.

*"Seek"* (求) means to pray and beseech the gods. It may refer to others praying on one’s behalf or to one’s own prayers. Whether one has descendants is decreed by Heaven—can it truly be sought? Yet among the three forms of unfilial conduct, having no posterity is the gravest. One cannot but seek descendants. Moreover, humans are born of blood and qi; all beings with blood and qi can procreate. Some may have depleted their yin virtue, incurring Heaven’s displeasure; others may suffer from misaligned yang and yin, their fate marked by solitude; still others may have deficiencies in the womb or self-inflicted harm from desire and anger—thus remaining childless. Yet human determination can reverse Heaven’s decree. Sincerity moves the divine; there is none who cannot have descendants. Thus, descendants may indeed be sought.

*"Descendants"* (嗣息): *"Si"* means to continue; some say it means *"to connect"* (系), as in continuing the ancestral line like a linked chain. Others say *"si"* means *"to administer"* (司), as in managing the family’s legacy and ensuring the transmission of its records. *"Xi"* refers to children who carry on the lineage, like the ceaseless breath of inhalation and exhalation. Moreover, the true breath of a person is the root of life and death; when breath ceases, qi is cut off. Having children ensures the ancestral sacrifices continue unbroken—hence the term *"xi."* Fundamentally, *"xi"* originates from the heart. With a child, all things are fulfilled, and this heart may rest at ease—thus it is called *"xi."*

The sages of the Three Teachings all had children. Why do people not recognize the weight of descendants?

*"In accordance with the proper method and with veneration"* refers to revering the scripture. Some mistakenly interpret this as self-veneration. *"Celestial bureaus"* (天曹) are those who govern destiny and oversee the registers of immortals. The *"nine ranks"* consist of the upper three, middle three, and lower three grades, totaling nine. The *Scriptural Exegesis* states: *"The nine grades of lotus flowers serve as parents."* In Buddhism, the nine ranks include Buddhas, bodhisattvas, those of perfect enlightenment, voice-hearers, arhats, and the four dhyanas.

The *"Four Fruits of Immortals"* are *Srotaāpanna*, *Sakṛdāgāmin*, *Anāgāmin*, and *Arhat*. *"Descending to be born"* (降谪下生) means immortals and Buddhas incarnating in the human realm, hence *"descending."* *"Talent"* (才) refers to exceptional skill; *"eloquence"* (辩) to unparalleled verbal acuity.

The radiance of illumination shines upon all without exception. The wisdom of awakening comprehends all without obstruction. Among humankind, the most revered and noble are not merely kings, ministers, or teachers, but those who embody virtue and the Dao—perfected beings and true heirs. Thus, nothing is more revered than the Dao, nothing more noble than virtue. Even the Son of Heaven cannot enfeoff them as subjects, nor can feudal lords befriend them as equals. They assist in the unfolding of creation, shaping and sustaining all things, their legacy venerated for ten thousand generations, their supremacy acknowledged across the Three Realms. Hence, they are called the most revered and noble among humankind.

To revere this scripture in accordance with the Dharma, whether reciting it oneself or having others do so to seek descendants, though its efficacy is manifest, one must not expect results within a set time. Only by honoring the scripture, repenting of past transgressions, and maintaining unwavering devotion—treating this scripture as though beholding the Jade Emperor himself, and revering this Dharma-treasure with utmost sincerity—will the divine verses and numinous power silently transform fate, altering destiny and ensuring descendants. Even worldly meritorious officials may secure blessings for their offspring. How much more so for those who, as true believers in the scripture and propagators of the Dharma-wheel, become the meritorious ministers of Heaven and Earth? Shall they not ensure the prosperity of their descendants? If one reveres this scripture yet fails to beget heirs, it must be that their sincerity is insufficient or their devotion inconsistent with the Dharma. Let the people of the world take heed and be diligent, and they shall witness the scripture’s divine efficacy for themselves.

**Chapter on Meritorious Virtues (continued, twenty-fourth section):**
When a woman approaches the perilous month of childbirth and recites this true scripture in accordance with the Dharma, she shall secure the well-being of both mother and child, giving birth to sons or daughters of blessed fortune and virtue, beloved and revered by all. The scripture’s divine power extends far beyond merely ensuring descendants.

When a woman in the perilous month of childbirth recites this true scripture in accordance with the Dharma, she shall promptly and safely deliver the child, free from distress, bearing sons or daughters of auspicious appearance and virtuous character, cherished and esteemed by all. A woman who has married into her husband’s family is called a *matron*. The *perilous month* refers to the time of childbirth, when life hangs in the balance, perilously close to death. To *recite the true scripture in accordance with the Dharma* means that, whether before labor begins—through her own devout recitation—or during labor—by having others recite the scripture—she shall ensure the safety of both mother and child. At the appointed hour of delivery, there shall be no complications; the child shall be born without delay, the mother unharmed, and the infant delivered smoothly, both thriving in peace and well-being, mutually benefiting.

*Fortune* refers to those endowed with wealth, nobility, health, longevity, and auspicious signs. *Virtue* refers to those who are sagely, wise, loyal, righteous, filial, fraternal, and morally upright. *Sons or daughters* may be either male or female, both possessing fortune and virtue. *Beloved and revered by all* means they are endearing yet not to be treated with familiarity, approachable yet not to be trifled with. People delight in their company, praise them, and admire them—this is *love*. Yet at the mere sight of them, awe arises; in their presence, indolence vanishes. Though they speak not, they are trusted; though they act not, they are revered—this is *respect*.

**Resolving Doubts:**
That men are beloved and revered is understandable. But how can women be so? If a woman possesses a fully auspicious appearance, exemplary conduct, neither weeping nor frivolity, solemnity and reticence, minimal movement or laughter, displaying maturity in youth and dignity in age, maintaining purity until death, people will cherish her as kin and revere her as an elder. Such love is free from impropriety; it arises from admiration for her rare fortune and virtue.

**Verification:**
Safety of mother and child.

From the *"Shengmin"* chapter of the *Book of Songs*, in praise of Jiang Yuan’s birth of Hou Ji: "The firstborn came forth like a lamb, free from harm or disaster." As Jiang Yuan possessed virtue, her first birth was as effortless as that of a lamb, untouched by misfortune—mother and child both secure. The exegetical tradition states: "Mother and child part early from the body." The *Daoist Canon* declares: "At the moment of delivery, there is joy; in the pangs of labor, no peril." The merit of reciting scriptures responds as swiftly as an echo.

Today, the *August Scripture* circulates widely in the world, and those who recite it are many. Yet when women give birth, some miscarry before their time; some suffer calamity, peril, or death; some bear ghostly fetuses or monstrous anomalies; some face karmic retribution or mutual affliction. Though many mothers and children remain safe, they cannot always both be preserved; in some cases, neither fares well. Some doubt the efficacy of recitation, unaware that the true Dharma is rarely encountered. Among the people, some have never heard of this scripture’s exalted status or its benefits; others have heard its name but fail to revere it. Some offer empty reverence without recitation; others recite but begin with zeal and end in neglect. Some omit passages or jumble them, or defile and invert them, miswriting characters and misplacing phrases, misconstruing their meaning. Though they may include texts of repentance and gratitude, they cannot attain the full merit of the scripture. All such errors are self-inflicted; failing to encounter the scripture’s power, they conclude that recitation is futile—this is slander of the Dharma.

Alas! The true Dharma should be practiced, and the debt to one’s parents is hard to repay. To rescue the suffering and benefit the living is the urgent duty of those who study the Dao. If one teaches the world to revere and recite this scripture, mothers and children will be safe, the perils of childbirth averted, and good sons and daughters born. The world will be filled with those of fortunate and virtuous countenance, and the true Dao will endure forever. The merit is immeasurable. Let those who study this scripture take heed.

*Collected Commentaries on the August Scripture, Volume Six* concludes.
*Collected Commentaries on the August Scripture, Volume Seven* begins.

Collated by the Fifty-First Celestial Master of the Han Succession, Great Perfected Zhang Guoxiang.
Reviewed by the Recluse Beyond the Court, Luo Hongxian of Jishui, Jiangxi, former *Zhuangyuan*.
Compiled by Zhou Xuanchen of Xiaozhaochen, Jinan, Shandong.
*Merit Section*, continued from Chapter Twenty-Five.

Should one seek office and promotion, desiring rank and emolument to flourish, and noble audience with the sovereign—if one upholds and recites this true scripture in accordance with the proper method—then one shall obtain advancement in office and duty, with descendants honored and exalted, their prosperity unbroken through the generations. How could the benefits of this Jade Scripture be limited merely to safe childbirth?

To seek office is to obtain position where none existed before. To advance in rank: *advance* means to ascend; when one’s post is humble, one strives for promotion to higher and more distinguished office. *Rank* refers to the titles conferred upon officials; *emolument* denotes the stipends they receive. *Flourish* signifies joyous ease, upright felicity, and propitious profit; *attain* means the fulfillment of one’s aspirations and the success of one’s plans. *Noble audience with the sovereign*: to rise above the common is to be noble; when rank and emolument flourish, one may further receive the sovereign’s governance and the ruler’s favor. The meeting of ruler and minister has ever been rare. Only when nobility is matched by audience with the sovereign does the minister’s elevation become truly meaningful—this is what it means to attain. Some say it refers to encountering a wise and enlightened ruler. The phrase *this true* is not redundant; the syntax requires it.

*Office and duty*: an office entails both administrative authority and specific responsibilities, each with its own jurisdiction—hence *office*. *Duty* refers to governmental affairs. *Transfer* means to be reassigned to a more desirable post, or to move from an idle sinecure to a position of influence. *Descendants honored and exalted*: this may entail hereditary office and emolument, or the conferral of rank upon one’s children and grandchildren, ensuring enduring wealth, honor, and prosperity that illumines the past and enriches the future—both honor and nobility enduring unbroken through the generations. Though not equal to the permanence of heaven and earth, such blessings flow to posterity, their fortune and grace extending unbroken in an unending line. Even if limited to several generations, it is still called long-lasting. Consider the supreme virtue of Confucius or the divine transformations of the Ancestral Celestial Master—through the ages, their descendants received imperial enfeoffments, true honor and nobility unceasing.

Enfeoffment, rank, and stipends derive from the imperial court’s official system. If they cannot be sought, how does one advance through the ranks? If they cannot be schemed for, then—whether in civil or military service—one must study the classics or master strategy, accumulating learning and merit, for these cannot be borrowed. How, then, can one seek advancement? The principle is this: office is conferred upon the virtuous, and emolument sustains the worthy. Those of great virtue must attain their rightful position and renown.

If one upholds this true scripture in accordance with the proper method, reciting its divine words and adhering to its sagely teachings, aligning one’s heart with heaven and moving in harmony with the Dao, one becomes a person of great goodness. Whether through eloquent speech or exemplary conduct, one may be recommended for office despite lacking rank, or granted emolument despite holding no post; or, if already in office, one may be promoted. This is the natural principle of employing the worthy. When virtue draws forth recognition, even if one seeks it not—like Guangcheng, Yin Shou, Chaoyou, or Yan Guang—one may become an imperial teacher or a guest of the state, though one may decline. How, then, can it be said that the good cannot obtain rank and emolument? Consider Zhuge Liang of the Han, or Jingyang, Li Bi, and Hai Chan of the Jin and Tang—all attained office through the cultivation of the Dao. The scripture speaks of seeking office; why doubt it?

In essence, the celestial rank of inherent nobility resides within us all. To transcend the mundane and enter sainthood is a gradual ascent in virtue. Through divine transformation, one ascends to immortality, enduring through the kalpas unharmed. As the *Daodejing* says: "Though the body dies, it does not perish." The immortals’ traditions declare: "Children beget grandchildren, and grandchildren beget descendants." The Dao is revered, virtue is exalted, and the transmission of immortality is endless. Those who do not covet worldly fame or fleeting profit, but cultivate the celestial rank of inherent nobility—progressing from the mortal to the sagely, transcending the dust to ascend as immortals—shall perpetuate their lineage forever. This is the true fruit of upholding this true scripture in accordance with the proper method.

*Merit Section, continued from Chapter Twenty-Six*

Should one desire abundant wealth and prosperity, and uphold this true scripture in accordance with the proper method, one shall obtain treasures in overflowing abundance, with clothing and sustenance naturally provided, blessings flowing to descendants and transmitted to posterity. How could the benefits of this scripture be limited merely to the fulfillment of one’s ambitions for office?

Should one desire abundant household wealth and resources, and uphold this true scripture in accordance with the proper method, one shall obtain an overflowing abundance of goods, treasures, and luxuries, with clothing and food pure and plentiful, naturally abundant. The remaining blessings shall flow to descendants and be transmitted to posterity.

*Resources* refers to what is needed; the necessities of oneself and one’s family are called *resources*. Some say it means *to rely upon*, signifying that people depend on these for their livelihood—equivalent to *capital*. *Wealth* refers to goods and property, or the means of sustenance. *Abundant* means substantial, the quality of being fully provided. *Prosperous* describes a household in the utmost plenty, with wealth and resources in full sufficiency.

*Uphold*: to revere is to *uphold*; to maintain, observe, and recite. *Treasures*: *wealth* refers to goods and property; *treasures* are valuable items such as gold, silver, pearls, jade, rarities, curios, fine fabrics—all things rare, precious, and esteemed. *Overflowing* means full to excess; *abundant* means plentiful. *Clothing and sustenance naturally provided*: without expending effort, one enjoys natural abundance. *Blessings flowing to descendants*: *blessings* are auspicious signs; when fortune and grace extend far, passing through generations in unbroken succession, it is called *blessings flowing to descendants*. *Transmitted to posterity*: not limited to one generation’s children and grandchildren, but passed down through the ages, so that later generations forever enjoy peace and prosperity.

*Resolving Doubts*:
Confucius said: "If wealth could be sought, though it required holding a whip as a lowly servant, I would do it. If it cannot be sought, I shall follow what I love." This strongly suggests that wealth cannot be sought—how, then, can this scripture say that if one desires abundance and upholds it in accordance with the proper method, one shall attain wealth and blessings flowing to posterity? Does this not imply that wealth *can* be sought?

Zixia said: "I have heard that wealth and nobility are decreed by heaven." Though wealth may be desirable, fate determines it, and heaven truly governs it—how, then, can it be sought? Yet while wealth may be fated, the early Confucians said: "Wealth, nobility, fortune, and grace enrich one’s end, as reward for virtue." To uphold this scripture in accordance with the proper method, reciting its celestial words and revering the august heavens, is to be one whose virtue earns blessing.

The *Book of Songs* says: "Joyful is the noble man—blessings and grace sustain him." This is precisely what is meant. Yet some argue: "Great Yu was a sage, yet he drank plainly, wore coarse clothes, and dwelt in a humble palace, content with frugality and restraint." The Daoists say: "To study the Dao, one must embrace bone-deep poverty." Such claims make the assertion that upholding this scripture brings wealth seem like a lure for personal gain. But Yu’s self-denial was the heart of a sage, free from selfish attachment. The Daoist pursuit of poverty was a mind unburdened by material things. That wealth may be sought and attained is but a demonstration of the scripture’s efficacy. The Buddhist sutras say: "All that is sought shall be fulfilled." Both teachings open wide their gates of expedient means, offering boundless blessings and benefits—every prayer answered, every wish granted. Yet if doubt lingers, if effort wavers from diligence to neglect, if the heart’s resolve is flawed, lacking steadfast faith and perfect sincerity, then even seeking will yield nothing. Those who study this scripture must take this to heart.

I have long observed that all things are complete within us, and inherent wealth requires no external acquisition. Why, then, do people chase fame and profit, vie for fleeting splendor, and indulge in desires, mistaking the false for the true? The poor grieve, unable to uphold the great Dao or recite the true scripture. The rich live in excess, unwilling to abandon cunning gain or honor the sacred words, content to age and die, forfeiting their innate abundance. Laozi said: "Gold and jade fill the hall, yet none can keep them; wealth and pride invite their own downfall." This warns against pursuing the external while neglecting the inner. Now, to speak of the scripture’s power to bring wealth is to redirect the heart’s pursuit toward reverence for the scripture. Ah! Upholding it truly yields results—but without sincerity, one invites only fault. Those who study this scripture must be cautious.

**Chapter on Merits, Continued (27)**
If one is afflicted by malign stars, suffering hardship and confinement to bed, and upholds this true scripture according to the proper method, those malign stars will reverse their course and bestow auspicious blessings. The benefits of this scripture extend far beyond the attainment of wealth. If one’s fate in a given year is overshadowed by malign stars, bringing distress, restless sleep, and affliction, reciting this true scripture with due reverence will transform misfortune into fortune, turning those malign stars into harbingers of good fortune.

Red Pine said: "All people are solitary, each bearing a star." Yet the stars’ influence may be auspicious or inauspicious—auspicious stars bring a thousand blessings, while inauspicious ones foretell calamity. Those under the light of the Three Luminaries must ever be vigilant. Malign stars are inauspicious stars, such as Rahu, Ketu, the Star of Ruin, the Star of Wailing, the Star of Defeat, and the Star of Retreat. Their "overshadowing" may occur in one’s natal chart, annual fate, or the astrological division of one’s dwelling—wherever their influence falls, it is fixed and inescapable. "Distress" means thwarted aspirations, stifled and unfulfilled. "Suffering" may be poverty, illness, or peril pressing upon the body. "Distress and confinement to bed" describe utter helplessness, restless sleep, and unceasing agitation—some say it portends startling dreams. The term "malign stars" encompasses all inauspicious celestial bodies whose influence, whether in fate, annual fortune, or dwelling, brings misfortune.

Yet when one upholds this scripture with proper devotion, its divine verses transform ill omens into blessings. Though malign stars typically bring only misfortune, the scripture’s power ensures that those under their sway not only avoid calamity but receive hidden blessings and good fortune. The stars’ influence in fate or dwelling is fixed—how, then, can reciting the scripture turn malign stars auspicious? Heaven and humanity share a single principle, and the realms above and below are interconnected. The mechanism of resonance is swifter than an echo. When Duke Xiang of Song spoke three words of humane governance, the planet Mars retreated from its position. Nor is this power reserved for rulers: a faithful widow’s grievance brought three years of drought, and a lowly servant’s remorse summoned frost in June. Even common men and women can move heaven and earth, stir gods and spirits—provided their sincerity is unfeigned. With perfect sincerity, a single word can win the trust of the divine, and silence itself can alter the workings of creation. How much more so this true scripture, the essence of heaven and earth, by which the Three Luminaries attain their radiance! To uphold it with reverence, to honor its sacred verses and profound mysteries, is to scatter inauspicious stars, summon good fortune, and turn calamity into blessing.

Human nature is one: following the good brings fortune, defying it brings misfortune. The *Scripture of Central Admonitions* says: "The stars’ influence—auspicious or inauspicious—is summoned by one’s own deeds. Those who do good shine with starlight; great goodness shines brightly. Those who do neither good nor evil glow faintly; minor transgressions scatter their light; grave sins extinguish it." When malign stars descend, their retribution falls upon the guilty; the blameless may escape punishment, while the virtuous turn misfortune into fortune. As the saying goes: "A single evil thought becomes a malevolent star or vengeful ghost; a single good thought becomes a propitious star or auspicious cloud." Fortune and misfortune hinge on the goodness or evil of a single thought. To truly uphold this scripture is to dispel even the gravest disasters and calamities—how much more so the distress of confinement or fleeting misfortune? King Tai Jia said: "Heaven-sent disasters may yet be averted." This is the same principle.

When one can magnify the Dao and assist in creation, silently reciting the true scripture to penetrate the divine and master transformation, one’s virtue aligns with heaven and earth, one’s brightness with the sun and moon, one’s order with the four seasons, and one’s fortune with the gods and spirits. Mastering creation, one transcends the mortal realm, eluding the gaze of ghosts and spirits, untouched by life and death—how then could stars constrain one’s fate? Let those who study this scripture take heed.

**Chapter on Merits, Continued (28)**
If one’s life has ended and they are bound for hell, their name recorded in the ledgers of the damned, their parents, teachers, spouse, or children may recite this scripture on their behalf, or establish a ritual altar with banners and offerings. Thus, the deceased’s name is erased from the ghostly registers, their spirit reborn in the Pure Land, and all suffering souls are delivered. By this supreme merit, all ascend to heaven.

The merits of this scripture not only absolve one’s faults in life, but when a person’s allotted span has ended and their soul is bound for hell—with their name inscribed in the ledgers of evil—then their parents, teachers, spouse, sons, or daughters should recite this scripture on their behalf or establish a ritual altar for deliverance, adorned with banners and offerings. Thus, the deceased’s name is erased from the ghostly registers, their spirit reborn in the Pure Land, and all those who share the same hell, the same death, or the same ledger of suffering are likewise redeemed by the scripture’s power, ascending to heaven through this most sublime and wondrous Dharma benefit.

When it is said that one’s *allotted span has ended*, it means their destined years have passed and they die. *Bound for hell* refers to those who, whether in past lives or this one, have committed sins and must enter hell to receive their karmic retribution. *Hell* encompasses realms such as Fengdu, the Iron Enclosure, the Nine Hells, Eighteen Hells, Twenty-Four Hells, Thirty-Six Hells, Seventy-Two Hells, and the Avīci Hell. *Inscribed in the ledgers of evil* means their name is recorded in the registers of wickedness to face retribution. *Parents and teachers* may refer to the deceased’s own parents, their mentors, or elders. *Spouse, sons, or daughters* indicates that whether the deceased was a man or woman, they would have had a spouse; if not, then children or other kin would remain. *Should recite this scripture on their behalf* means the living relatives must uphold and chant this scripture for the departed. *Or establish a ritual altar* refers to arranging a sacred space to follow the Daoist rites and liberate the deceased from sin, hence called a *ritual altar*. *Banners and offerings*: Banners, inscribed with endless praises of the sacred names, are hung from tall poles so the wind may carry their radiant light in all directions. *Flowers* are the finest blossoms offered to welcome the divine. In heaven, the spreading of the Dharma is naturally accompanied by banners, canopies, and pennants, as well as resplendent celestial flowers, all manifesting auspicious brilliance. In the mortal realm, banners are merely placed in the ritual space to adorn it and invoke divine favor. *Offerings*: *Offer* means to present, while *nourish* refers to providing food and drink. In heaven, the purest and most wondrous Dharma is the true sustenance, and the celestial kitchens’ sacred offerings are given only with reverence and devotion. In this world, the five offerings and ten offerings—though they may include the emptying of the five aggregates, the removal of the five sufferings, the restraint of the five pleasures, the abandonment of the ten evils, the contemplation of the ten truths, and the abiding in the ten faiths—are ultimately meant to honor the radiant deities, revere the true Dharma, and uphold the sacred scriptures. This is the true offering. Yet today, people merely present food, flowers, fruits, and rare treasures without genuine reverence—this is but a superficial practice. The *offerings* spoken of in this scripture are those of sincere and natural devotion.

*Thus, the deceased’s name is erased from the ghostly registers, their spirit reborn in the Pure Land.* By reciting this scripture once or establishing a ritual altar, the departed are freed from the dark underworld, their black karma dissolved, their names stricken from the ledgers of evil, and their spirits reborn in the Pure Land of bliss. All those who share the same hell, the same death, or the same ledger of suffering are likewise delivered by the compassionate power of this scripture. *All* means everyone. *Ascending to heaven through this sublime Dharma benefit*: *This* refers to the merit of the scripture. Though heavenly blessings may eventually wane, a single recitation of this true scripture—or the establishment of a ritual altar—erases the sins of the deceased and those suffering in hell, allowing them to be reborn in heaven. If one upholds it perpetually, how could its merits ever be exhausted? Alas, hell has no gate—it is man who invites it; heaven has a path, yet the deluded cannot walk it. Moreover, the original nature of life is the true scripture of the Imperial Dao. To uphold and recite it always is to remain unstained by hell or heaven, forever attaining the mysterious truth.

Thus, those who diligently uphold this scripture:

**Chapter on Merits, Continued (29)**
Wherever this scripture resides, the Ten Heavenly Perfected Great Saints and the Boundless Flying Celestial Kings shall attend and make offerings. Those who uphold this scripture may rightfully call themselves *Orthodox Unity Perfected Ones*. Wherever they dwell, the Vajra Warriors of the Ten Directions, the most true and sacred, shall secretly guard them as they would their own bodies. Whether going out or coming in, wherever they travel, all evil spirits will flee, demons and ghosts will be eradicated, malevolent spirits will hide in submission, and no calamity or misfortune shall draw near. When such a scripture-holder’s life is about to end, they will no longer witness the dreadful visions of hell but will instead see celestial maidens from the heavenly palaces descending with banners to welcome them, and they shall be reborn in heaven. When their heavenly blessings are exhausted and they descend to the human realm, for a thousand lives and ten thousand lives they shall always be kings or great ministers, blessed with sagely rulers and wise counselors, their lands at peace, their people content, and their innate nature ever luminous. They shall follow the Great Dao, advancing in cultivation until they reach the shore of the Dao.

Such are the blessings bestowed upon those who uphold this scripture. How could its merits benefit only the living and the dead? Wherever this scripture resides, the Ten Heavenly Perfected Great Saints and the Boundless Flying Celestial Kings shall always attend and make offerings. Those who uphold this scripture may rightfully call themselves *Orthodox Unity Perfected Ones*. Wherever they dwell, the Vajra Warriors of the Ten Directions, the most true and sacred, shall secretly guard them as they would their own bodies. Whether going out or coming in, wherever they travel, all evil spirits will retreat, the path they walk will be cleansed of demons and ghosts, malevolent spirits will hide in submission, and no calamity or misfortune shall draw near. When such a scripture-holder’s life is about to end, they will no longer witness the dreadful visions of hell but will instead see celestial maidens from the heavenly palaces descending with banners and regalia to welcome them, and they shall be reborn in heaven. When their heavenly blessings are exhausted and they descend to the human realm, for a thousand lives and ten thousand lives they shall always be kings or great ministers, blessed with sagely rulers and wise counselors, their lands at peace, their people content, and their innate nature ever luminous. They shall follow the Great Dao, advancing in cultivation until they reach the shore of the Dao.

The scripture-holder who thus receives such blessings shall be favored in this manner. The Ten Heavens and the ten directions’ hosts of heavens—flying celestial spirit-kings, those divine kings who soar and roam in protection of the heavens—along with their retinues standing in attendance and guard, the Perfected of Orthodox Unity (those of utmost rectitude and impartiality, true and undivided in oneness), and the vajra-enforcers of both teachings, all come to safeguard the scripture-holder. They secretly shield this person, guarding them in concealment as if protecting their own divine bodies, so earnest is the care of these spirits. Wherever the scripture-holder goes—whether departing or entering, whether in travel or repose—all demonic beings, malevolent spirits, ghosts, and specters flee and hide in submission, utterly eradicated and banished. Calamity, misfortune, and disaster cannot draw near.

When such a person’s life comes to an end, they shall no longer witness the dreadful visions of hell in all their forms. Celestial maidens from the heavenly palaces—*not* to be mistaken as "heavenly officials" (天官)—descend bearing the great Dharma-banners, neither flags nor pennants, to welcome and guide them upward to heaven. For a thousand lives and ten thousand lives, they shall be kings and great ministers, ever noble through the ages, just as the Buddha was a royal prince for eighteen thousand lifetimes, the Dark Emperor was a king for a thousand lifetimes and a chief minister for another thousand, and Lord Lao served as the teacher of emperors and kings across kalpas. They shall dwell in lasting peace and joy, their innate nature ever luminous, their great wisdom and profound insight undimmed by the good causes of past kalpas. They shall uphold, revere, and follow the Great Dao, advancing in cultivation through repeated practice until they reach the shore of the Dao—attaining the Dao as one crosses to the far shore of the sea. The Buddha spoke of "arriving at the other shore," and the *Greater Elegantiae* says: "He first ascended to the shore." This is what is meant. Some call it the "bank of the Dao."

The merit of upholding this scripture brings benefit at every moment, whether for the living or the dead, for few or many, for long or short durations—all wishes shall be fulfilled, and its transformative power is truly divine. Through thousands and tens of thousands of lifetimes, unwavering devotion and repeated cultivation will at last lead one to the shore of the Dao. Thus is the difficulty of great attainment made clear. Those who study this scripture must steel their resolve, for they may enter the most exalted ranks.

**Chapter on Merits, Continued (Thirty-One)**

If one who upholds this scripture, when rain falls, arouses great compassion and, in accordance with the Dharma, recites these three scrolls once toward the empty sky, then wherever the rain touches and in the direction it faces, all sentient beings—those guilty of the five rebellions, the ten evils, and all grave transgressions—shall have their sins wholly dissolved. All serious illnesses shall naturally be cured. After the lives of these beings end, they shall not fall into hell but shall be reborn in the Pure Land, transformed from lotus flowers. How much more so for those who uphold this scripture!

If one who upholds this scripture, while traveling on the road, encounters a great wind that blows upon them, then wherever the dust from their body touches, all sentient beings shall have their evil karma completely eradicated. They shall no longer fall into the three evil destinies but shall be reborn in the heavens. Thus is the merit of upholding this scripture beyond all conception.

The merit of this scripture does not benefit only the individual. If one who upholds this scripture, when rain falls, arouses a heart of great compassion to deliver the world and, in accordance with the Dharma, recites these three scrolls—upper, middle, and lower—once toward the empty sky, then wherever the rain moistens and in the direction it faces, all sentient beings—those guilty of the five rebellions, the ten evils, and all grave transgressions—shall have their sins wholly dispersed and eradicated. All serious illnesses shall naturally be healed and restored to health. After the lives of these beings in the direction the scripture-holder faces end, they shall not fall into hell but shall be reborn in the Pure Land, free from defilement, transformed from lotus flowers. How could the merit of those who uphold this scripture be measured?

If one who upholds this scripture, while traveling on the road, encounters a great wind that blows upon them, then wherever the dust from their body touches, all sentient beings shall have their evil karma completely eradicated. They shall no longer fall into the three evil destinies but shall be reborn in the heavens. Thus is the merit of upholding this scripture beyond all conception.

**Textual Verification and Doubt Resolution:**
The scripture-holder is compassionate in all places, not only when rain falls or wind rises. The phrase "when rain falls or wind rises" is used to emphasize the efficacy of upholding the scripture. "Great compassion" is the deep pity and concern for all beings in the world. As the Buddhist teachings say, "the heart of compassion is too fervent." The *Book of Songs* says: "Pity these solitary and suffering ones," and also: "Have compassion for those who have not become whole." The Daoist scriptures say: "Great compassion awakens the world." The scripture-holder, moved by the suffering of beings bound by karma, arouses great compassion.

The five rebellions are: disloyalty, unfiliality, lack of kindness, discord, and injustice. Another interpretation holds them to be: disrespect for Heaven and Earth, ruler and parents, and teachers; disrespect for the Three Treasures; failure to uphold precepts; neglect of virtuous conduct; and ignorance of the nature of mind. Yet another interpretation sees them as indulgence in wealth, sensuality, fame, food, and sleep.

The ten evils are: killing, stealing, sexual misconduct, divisive speech, false speech, frivolous speech, harsh speech, greed, anger, and delusion.

"All serious illnesses shall be cured." *Cured* means restored to health; *healed* means returned to wholeness.

"Transformed from lotus flowers": In Buddhist teaching, this refers to rebirth in the Western Pure Land of Ultimate Bliss, where beings are born from lotus flowers. In Daoist teaching, it refers to the Azure Flower Realm of Eternal Joy, where beings are transformed from lotus flowers. Another saying holds that desire arises from the lotus flower, meaning that in both worldly and transcendent existence, one remains unstained by a single speck of dust, and the wondrous Dharma gives rise to life without defiled thoughts. It is also likened to the primordial state of Heaven and Earth, where beings are naturally transformed into existence.

Confucian scholars often dismiss these two teachings as delusion, yet was Yi Yin, born from a hollow mulberry, not a sage of the Confucian tradition? Moreover, the human heart is like the lotus—understanding the lotus’s birth reveals the seal of the mind’s essence. How excellent that Master Zhou, in observing the lotus, not only admired its purity amid mud but must also have had a deeper insight. Thus he became a patriarch of the Learning of the Dao.

The three evil destinies are hell, demonic spirits, and animals. "Beyond conception" means beyond the reach of thought or speech, signifying that the merit is immeasurable.

The Dao’s deliverance of the world and the true scripture’s benefit to humanity are like the soaking rain that nourishes all, resounding through the ten directions and benefiting the entire Dharma realm. The Confucian tradition says: "There are those transformed as if by timely rain," and also: "The wind in the trees makes its sound," and further: "Not swift yet speedy, passing through and transforming, abiding and divine—people daily turn toward goodness without knowing who effects it." Thus when the Duke of Zhou was born, there were no violent winds or excessive rains, and even beyond the seas, people turned toward civilization. If one can ultimately attain the Dao and uphold the true scripture, then whether touched by rain or wind, or even where wind and rain do not reach and water and dust do not touch, the three thousand great thousand worlds shall forever be bathed in the benefit of the Dharma. Thus it is said that the merit of upholding this scripture is beyond conception.

**Chapter on Merits, Thirty-One Continued**

If one who upholds this scripture crosses rivers, the Huai, or the sea, and bathes in their waters, then wherever the water from their body touches, all beings within—fish, turtles, soft-shelled turtles, and all aquatic creatures—shall have their evil karma wholly eradicated for the remainder of this life. After their lives end, they shall no longer be reborn from wombs, eggs, moisture, or transformation.

All speech and sound uttered by one who upholds this scripture shall be pure Dharma sounds, and all demonic and heterodox beings who hear them shall be thus purified.

If one who upholds this scripture encounters temples of various spirits and recites this scripture for them, then upon hearing it, those spirits shall escape the ghostly realm and ascend to the attainment of immortality. They shall revere this person as if serving an emperor.

The merit of one who upholds this scripture extends not only to humans. When this scripture passes through rivers, the Huai, or the sea, and one bathes in their waters, wherever the water from their body touches, all beings within—fish, turtles, soft-shelled turtles, and all aquatic creatures—shall have the evil karma they accumulated in past lives wholly eradicated for the remainder of this present life. After their lives end, they shall attain wondrous fruition and no longer be reborn from wombs, eggs, moisture, or transformation.

All speech and sound uttered by one who upholds this scripture—whether demons or heterodox beings hear them—shall not be perceived as worldly words or sounds but as pure Dharma tones. If one who upholds this scripture encounters temples of various spirits and recites this scripture for them, those spirits, upon hearing it, shall escape the ghostly realm and ascend to the attainment of immortality. They shall revere this upholder of the scripture as if serving the Emperor who expounds it.

*Jingshe* (经涉): *Jing* means to pass through; *she* means to ford. One interpretation holds that *she* refers to crossing water on foot, as when Lü Dongbin chanted verses while flying over Dongting Lake or when Bodhidharma crossed the river on a reed. In essence, any crossing of water, whether on foot or by boat, is called *jingshe*. Rivers and seas are general terms for flowing waters. The Huai refers to the Huai River. *Muyu qishen* (沐浴其身) means to bathe one’s body in water. *Qizhong zhongsheng* (其中众生) refers to all living beings in the water. Those with scales are fish; those with shells are turtles, soft-shelled turtles, and the like. All aquatic life shall have their evil karma eradicated for this one lifetime alone. After death, they shall no longer cycle through the destinies of beasts—whether feathered, furred, scaled, shelled, or born of womb, egg, moisture, or transformation. Though humans are born of wombs, the term "womb" here does not refer to them but to other kinds. Even the dragon, lord of aquatic beings, is subject to karmic retribution; how much more so the lesser creatures below.

The true upholder of this scripture emanates golden light from body and speech. Thus, wherever their voice reaches, all demons and heterodox beings who hear it perceive only pure Dharma tones. When the scripture is recited for spirits, they escape the ghostly realm. Though spirits possess numinous power, manifest awe-inspiring radiance, and display karmic responses, they remain bound by their condition. Upon hearing this true scripture, their root consciousness is liberated, their karmic tendencies uprooted—what is called "dissolving all past habits." They ascend to the attainment of immortality, the highest realization of the true immortal’s Way.

The distinction between spirits and immortals lies in their station. Though spirits may possess merit, their glory and achievements remain manifest; even great spirits are thus constrained. They resemble noble sages who have not yet transcended form and trace, or those who have entered sagehood yet remain unrefined. Immortals, however, empty the five aggregates, purify the nine consciousnesses, transcend subject and object, and merge principle and phenomena. Though divided into five ranks, they stand far above spirits and ghosts.

When the sage emerges, all things flourish: mountains do not crumble, rivers do not dry, and birds, beasts, fish, and turtles all thrive in harmony. The dharma-water of the scripture-holder’s bath bestows its blessings upon aquatic beings, surpassing even the transformative power of a sagely king. When dragons receive the Dharma and awaken to profound insight, and when the dharma-water equally touches all, how could they not transcend? This is how the wild fox, upon receiving the Dharma, regained a human form. When the scripture is expounded to deliver spirits and ghosts from their condition—like Master Sa who delivered Marshal Wang or Master Pu who delivered Lord Guan—it causes them to revere divine majesty and forget past attachments. Thus, to this day, the reverence of these two sages has not waned.

**Chapter on Merit, Continuation of the Thirty-Second Section**

If one, while alive, fails to honor parents, disrespects the Three Treasures, kills, steals, engages in sexual misconduct, or utters falsehoods—committing the gravest of sins—and is on the verge of death, should a devout and faithful man or woman, moved by great compassion, recite the august name of the Jade Emperor seven times, fourteen times, twenty-one times, twenty-eight times, or even a hundred or a thousand times by the dying person’s side before their last breath fades, then all the unwholesome karma they accumulated in life shall be wholly eradicated. They shall not fall into any evil destinies but shall ascend to the Nine Heavens of Primordial Qi. How much more so for one who upholds this scripture?

Furthermore, if one has wandered through countless kalpas, drifting between heaven and the mortal realm, accumulating thousands of transgressions and myriad faults, and then encounters one who upholds this scripture—even if their shadow briefly falls upon them, as if embraced by the radiance of the Celestial Emperor, or if they converse with them or merely hear their voice, as if receiving the Celestial Emperor’s Dharma words of comfort—then that person’s karmic obstructions shall be forever eradicated.

The merit of this scripture extends not only downward to beings but upward to spirits and gods. If one, while alive, disrespects the Three Treasures, kills, steals, engages in sexual misconduct, or utters falsehoods—committing the gravest of sins—and is on the verge of death, should a devout and faithful man or woman, moved by great compassion, recite the august name of the Jade Emperor seven, fourteen, twenty-one, or up to a hundred or a thousand times by the dying person’s side before their last breath fades, then all the unwholesome karma they accumulated in life shall be wholly eradicated. They shall not fall into any evil destinies but shall ascend to the Nine Heavens of Primordial Qi. How much more so for one who upholds this scripture?

Furthermore, if one has wandered through countless kalpas, drifting between heaven and the mortal realm, accumulating thousands of transgressions and myriad faults, and then encounters one who upholds this scripture—even if their shadow briefly falls upon them, as if embraced by the radiance of the Celestial Emperor, or if they converse with them or merely hear their voice, as if receiving the Celestial Emperor’s Dharma words of comfort—then that person’s karmic obstructions shall be forever eradicated.

The Three Treasures are the Dao, the scriptures, and the masters. The Dao is the source of the Three Teachings and the origin of all existence; the scriptures are the bridge to salvation across the world.

The master is the eye and guide for humans and devas; to disrespect them is to incur one sin. Taking life, theft, sexual misconduct, and false speech—great transgressions bring great sins, yet even the mere sprouting of a single thought of these is sinful. Such offenses are manifold, not confined to one type, and words cannot exhaust their enumeration.

True faith is utmost sincerity, devoid of any ulterior motive. Whether reciting once in seven days, twice or thrice or four times in seven days, or a thousand or ten thousand times, or speaking of the Dao’s cyclical transformations of merit—all are valid. One does not fall into evil destinies, for one possesses good roots and harbors no perverse thoughts, thus avoiding such fates. Rebirth among humans and devas refers to the blessings of the human realm and heavenly realms. Some say human blessings constitute "human-deva" blessings. Drifting through the world’s domains describes the impermanence of life and death, like duckweed adrift on water, bound by karmic tendencies and difficult to escape, as if drowning in water. The world’s domains refer to the boundaries of the mundane realm.

When the shadow of one who upholds this scripture briefly falls upon a wrongdoer, it is as if the Celestial Emperor’s radiance has embraced and received them. *Shou* (受) should not be read as *shou* (授, "to bestow"), for it is not the Emperor’s light bestowing upon them. Generally, when such light descends upon a person, reverence arises spontaneously, and distracting thoughts vanish—hence it is called "embracing and receiving." We receive the Dharma benefits of that light.

Whether conversing with or merely hearing the voice of one who upholds the scripture—if the wrongdoer engages with them or hears their voice, the words of the Dao are profound and mysterious. Some say these are the words of the Dao Ancestor, but this interpretation is overly restrictive. *Wei yu* (慰谕) means to comfort with kind words and instruct with teachings and commands. The eradication of karmic obstructions means all sins from past lives and the present are released, and all hindrances from past kalpas and the present life are removed.

The scripture’s divine efficacy benefits both the living and the deceased, extending its blessings universally. Yet only those of true faith receive this merit. Thus, the foundation lies in utmost sincerity. If even the deceased are thus benefited, how immeasurable must be the merit of one who upholds this scripture? Let the reader take heed.

**Chapter 33 (Continued: Merits of Upholding the Scripture)**

Furthermore, if one who upholds this scripture fashions a long banner, inscribes the Emperor’s sacred name upon it, and hangs it from a tall pole—whether at a Daoist abbey or in a household courtyard—when the wind blows the banner toward any direction, all beings in that quarter shall partake of its sublime blessings, and all their evil karma shall be eradicated.

If one who upholds this scripture inscribes the Emperor’s sacred name upon any resonant object—be it bells, chimes, handbells, cymbals, or other ritual implements and instruments used in Daoist ceremonies—whether sounded in a ritual arena, struck in play, or set in motion by the wind, when the sound issues forth, near or far, all beings who hear it shall have their karmic obstructions purified. Here again is expounded the merit of upholding the scripture.

Long banners inscribed with the Emperor’s name, tall poles—both have been explained previously. *Guanyu* (观宇) refers to the halls within a Daoist abbey. *Guan* (观) means "abbey," where the spirit resides without distraction, for one’s mind is wholly fixed in sincere contemplation—hence it is called *guan*. *Jiating* (家庭) refers to the courtyard of a household. The direction the wind blows the banner is the quarter toward which it points. *Shengli* (胜利, "sublime blessings") has been explained earlier.

Bells, chimes, handbells, cymbals, and clappers are all ritual instruments made of metal. *All ritual implements and instruments* include those of metal, stone, silk, bamboo, gourd, clay, leather, and wood, as well as any resonant objects or tools used in Daoist ceremonies. In a ritual arena, sounding the Emperor’s name with such instruments is appropriate. But how can striking them in play also yield merit? This "play" does not refer to reckless or frivolous behavior. It may include practicing ritual music, or the actions of children or the ignorant who, unknowingly or mistakenly, sound ritual instruments or resonant objects, or those who, in leisure, take delight in playing such objects.

The "Emperor’s name" is not merely a personal name. The Emperor’s sacred title is imperishable across kalpas, an eternal and supreme name—hence it is called a "sacred name." Today, when we praise the Buddha’s "great name" or "sacred title," it carries the same meaning. When the wind touches resonant objects and sound issues forth, all beings near or far who hear it shall have their karmic obstructions purified.

**Textual Verification:**
The ancients created music to harmonize gods and humans, to unite high and low. How much more so for the Emperor’s sacred title, the glorious name of the Primordial Ancestor? Those who hear it are filled with reverence, and the vibration of the Dharma sound carries boundless divine efficacy. When the Great Shun composed music, even the beasts danced in unison—how much more so for humans? People grow solemn at the sound of weapons, amused at the sound of toys, and serene at the sound of the zither—how much more so for the Dharma sound? This applies to all resonant objects, not just ritual instruments. Ritual instruments are used to revere and commune with the divine, to unify the minds of the assembly. When the Emperor’s sacred name is inscribed upon them, those who hear it are moved to reverence, and their sins are extinguished.

Karmic obstructions are the retribution for the wicked. Upon hearing the Dharma sound, a virtuous heart stirs within, and thus all karmic obstructions are eradicated, and purity is attained. The sound of thunder, which inspires awe and reverence in those who hear it, serves as evidence.

**Resolving Doubts:**
In the present age, many hang banners and inscribe the Emperor’s sacred name upon ritual instruments and resonant objects, and many witness or hear them—yet their karmic obstructions remain. Why? Those who inscribe the name do not do so with the true and sincere heart of one who upholds the scripture. Those who witness or hear it regard it as commonplace, their obstinate minds unchanged, even to the point of disdain, frivolity, or blasphemy. Without a virtuous heart, how can sins be absolved?

Alas! The scripture and the Emperor’s sacred name possess immeasurable merit. First, one must uphold the scripture with true sincerity to transform oneself—then, without action, reverence arises; without words, faith is born. Only then, by propagating the Emperor’s sacred name to others, can one attain eternal benefit.

**Chapter 34 (Continued: Merits of Upholding the Scripture)**

Furthermore, if one who upholds this scripture awakens to the cycle of life and death, retreats deep into the mountains and forests to cultivate the Dao, and at times ascends to gaze upon the landscape—wherever their eyes alight, whether upon mountains, forests, streams, or valleys, all sentient beings—those with form and without, born from womb, egg, moisture, or transformation, crawling, flying, or hidden creatures of every kind—shall have their karmic offenses eradicated, their bodies and minds purified, and upon death, ascend to heaven.

How much more so for one who receives and upholds this scripture? Know that such a person is the very embodiment of the *Daozang*’s merit and power.

This further expounds the efficacy of upholding the scripture. To awaken to the cycle of life and death—life and death are matters of great consequence, their principles most difficult to fathom. We are born from the essence and blood of our parents, receiving the principle and *qi* of Heaven and Earth; yet why we live remains unknown. When essence is depleted and *qi* dispersed, spirit departs and form withers; yet why we die remains unknown. Though one may grasp the vicissitudes of affairs, observe the principles of things, speak with clarity, and act without delusion, without knowing life and death, one has not truly awakened. But if one who upholds this scripture severs attachment to sageliness and abandons cunning, removes the two obstacles, severs all delusions, dissolves karmic nature, attains great enlightenment, verifies the Three Origins, and ascends to the highest realm—awakening to the root of life and death—this is what the Buddhist school calls thorough awakening.

To retreat deep into the mountains and forests—of old, it was said: "The lesser hermit dwells in the mountains." Yet if one has awakened, whether on lofty peaks or level plains, all is a Daoist sanctuary; why must one enter the mountains and forests? The greater hermit dwells in the marketplace—this, too, is principle. But renowned mountains and sublime landscapes, where the land is numinous and people outstanding, are where true cultivators, though they may have no intent for mountain forests, find secluded peaks and lush groves, fashioned by Heaven and Earth as immortal realms. Thus, recluses enter therein.

To cultivate authenticity and study the Dao—humanity’s true nature is utterly genuine, without falsity; the nature of the Dao is spontaneous, not awaiting study. Yet as emotions and desires daily grow, knowledge is seduced and things transform, the genuine gradually becomes false, and the nature of the Dao obscured. Those who have awakened, in the mountains and beneath the forests, silently cultivate and return to celestial authenticity, studying the great Dao to liberate themselves from the cycle of life and death. The *Daode Jing* says: "Abandon learning, and there will be no sorrow." Yet here it speaks of studying the Dao—why? There, "abandoning learning" means forsaking the names, forms, and discriminations of conventional knowledge. Here, "studying the Dao" means learning the vast and effortless Way.

In the Buddhist school, thorough awakening is the highest attainment.
