They fall into the great hells, the Avīci Hell, with no end to their confinement. *Permanent* means final, eternal. Those who recklessly slander the true Dharma plant deep karmic roots, and thus their fall into hell makes escape nearly impossible.

All perception is one. If perception is not upright, it becomes perverse. All people are one; if they cling to delusion and fail to awaken, they become foolish and wicked. The heart is one; if it generates evil thoughts, it becomes rebellious. The eyes are one; if they cast biased, perverse glances, they become transgressive. A single thought, however minute, is one; if it gives rise to an evil intention, it becomes the root of karma. A single word, however slight, is one; if it utters an evil speech, it becomes the source of sin.

The noble person perfects the beauty of unity, commending others’ goodness. Those who uphold the scripture should be praised; to recklessly generate slander is to create one’s own suffering. Human life is originally free of karma, yet one invites grave sin upon oneself—how deeply lamentable.

The end of life is the return of the borrowed vessel of creation, naturally unbound. Yet for one who slanders the Dharma, even death brings no peace. How endless is their suffering, with no hope of escape.

The heart of Heaven is benevolent; the Supreme Deity cherishes life. The spirits are upright and do not abandon people. Yet if the sinner has no end to their confinement, is it Heaven that restrains them? No—calamity is self-made, and fixed karma is inescapable.

To dissolve one’s own karma, one must return to self-faith. Unwavering devotion to supreme goodness severs the root of karma. Alas! Heaven’s punishment is impartial; the law has no favorites. A compassionate father cannot shield his son, nor can a filial son protect his father. The spirits are perfectly just—how could they absolve self-inflicted karma?

Those who believe in the scripture and turn toward the Dharma may repent of past transgressions, just as a worldly person who confesses sincerely may be spared punishment. In this world, the sagely and the foolish are not inherently different. If one cannot speak with eloquence, let their voice at least follow the rhythm of the law; if one cannot avoid uttering false words, let them at least refrain from evil speech. If one cannot act without fault, let their body at least serve as a vessel for liberation; if one cannot avoid wrongdoing, let them at least refrain from creating bodily karma. If one cannot guard against delusive thoughts, let them at least preserve their utmost sincerity; if one cannot avoid evil intentions, let them at least refrain from creating mental karma.

If one does not understand the great Dao, let them not slander it. If one cannot comprehend the scripture, let them not reject it. If one cannot recite or uphold it, let them not slander others. If one does not understand spirits, life and death, or cause and effect, let them not utter defamatory words. With a heart of upright faith, clarity in the Dao will naturally arise over time, gradually attaining sagehood and transcendence—what karma or sin could then remain?

**Chapter Fourteen: Verification of Retribution Continued**

As for men and women in the world who encounter this scripture yet fail to revere it, handling it with filthy hands, reciting it with impure mouths, or placing it upon unclean beds and couches—those who, while reciting or expounding it, engage in vulgar speech and frivolous laughter, treating it as an ordinary text—such people, after their lives end, will fall into the Avīci Hell with no hope of escape. Their misfortune will extend to nine generations of ancestors, who will suffer interrogation in Fengdu. Their sins will burden their descendants, afflicting future generations.

In hell, they will endure immeasurable kalpas of immense suffering. Even if they encounter the sacred teachings and receive repeated salvation, their sins will only slightly diminish. If they further receive the extraordinary mercy of the highest sages, they may escape hell, only to be reborn among the hungry ghosts. For thousands of myriads of kalpas, they will not even hear the name of water or broth. Once their ghostly retribution is exhausted, they will be reborn as animals. After their animal retribution is complete, if they are reborn as humans, they will again appear in remote barbarian lands, as women, in poverty and hardship, afflicted with chronic illnesses and disabilities, enduring boundless suffering. They will be despised and rejected by all, unable to live in peace or die in ease. Such is the gravity of the sin incurred by treating this scripture lightly.

This is the retribution for disrespecting the scripture. *Failing to revere* means lacking respect and admiration. *Handling with filthy hands* refers to touching or flipping through the scripture with unclean hands, thereby defiling it. *Reciting with an impure mouth* means chanting or upholding it after consuming the five pungent vegetables, the five spices, or the three tabooed meats. *Placing it upon unclean beds or couches* means setting the scripture on dirty tables, seats, or platforms. *Engaging in vulgar speech and frivolous laughter* means discussing it in common language and treating it as a trivial text.

Such people not only bring sin upon themselves but also extend calamity to nine generations of ancestors—the four paternal uncles, the four maternal uncles, and the first ancestor. *Suffering interrogation in Fengdu* refers to the examination of transgressions, or the torture of interrogation. This reflects the principle that when a minor ghost commits a crime, the household head is held accountable. *Burdening descendants* means that the sins of an unvirtuous family linger, bringing endless suffering and affliction.

If they encounter the sacred teachings and receive repeated salvation, their sins may slightly diminish—this is the merit of the scripture and the Dharma. If they receive the extraordinary mercy of the highest sages, it is the pardon of the Supreme Deity, allowing them to escape the suffering of hell. Yet as hungry ghosts, they will endure thousands of myriads of kalpas without even hearing the name of water or broth. Hungry ghosts have vast bellies and narrow throats, making it impossible to consume food or drink. In the dark hells of Fengdu, they neither see nor hear of water or broth.

There is no creature more foolish than animals. Once their ghostly retribution is exhausted, they are reborn among them, their karma still unresolved. Though human existence is one, rebirth in the Central Kingdom is rare, and obtaining a male body is difficult. They will be born in remote barbarian lands, as women—among the least favored of human forms—enduring poverty, cold, hunger, and hardship, afflicted with chronic illnesses and disabilities, suffering boundless torment. They will be despised and rejected, unable to live in peace or die in ease—all due to their disrespect for this scripture. The gravity of their sin is truly lamentable.

Human life is one of the Three Powers, alongside Heaven and Earth. This body is precious, entrusted to us to continue our lineage, honor our ancestors, and benefit descendants. The Dao depends on it for transmission—its significance is not trivial. To slight the scripture and the Dharma brings calamity upon oneself, extends misfortune to ancestors, and burdens descendants with karmic retribution. How profoundly foolish is such a person?

Moreover, examining the texts of the Three Teachings, they declare that in the nature of the Dharma, there is no distinction between male and female—everywhere and in every place is a land of bliss. As the *Doctrine of the Mean* states: "In wealth or poverty, in high station or low, among barbarians or in times of peril, act according to one’s lot and find contentment in all circumstances." Yet why is it said that those in foreign lands as women, or those afflicted with illness and hardship, are despised and rejected by others?

The absence of distinction, where all find contentment, is the Dao. The differentiation of ranks and the discourse on sin and blessing are the workings of karmic retribution. When retribution is made clear, people are thus moved to goodness. Alas! All humans are one—all may become good people. Strive diligently to advance in the Dao, refrain from creating karma, and avoid being despised by others.

**Chapter Fifteen: Verification of Retribution**

There are also those who, though they initially believe and accept the teachings, later grow negligent and disdainful. The Youths of Good and Evil report to the Three Officials, inscribing their names in the Black Register and deducting their allotted years from the Azure Record. After death, they are confined in dark prisons, cycling through the Three Paths of suffering without hope of liberation. In this very life, they may endure all manner of illnesses—scabies, leprosy, ulcers, and abscesses—as karmic retribution, tormented day and night by sorrow, grief, and affliction. Or they may suffer violent harm, be shackled in prison, or meet unnatural deaths, poisoned and maimed. They may be devoured by tigers, wolves, or venomous serpents, or ensnared by karmic debts. While traversing mountains and forests, they may encounter brigands who slaughter them or cast them from cliffs. They may be harmed by malevolent spirits or specters, consumed by fire or swept away by floods, or slain indiscriminately by blades and weapons. For slighting this scripture, they forfeit their natural lifespan, reaping such consequences and such retribution—how can this not be lamentable? How can it not be agonizing?

The benefits of this scriptural Dharma are not such that only the utterly faithless incur sin. There are also those who, though they initially believe without doubt, later grow indolent and disrespectful. The Youths of Good and Evil—those who record the good and evil deeds of the world—report to the Three Officials, inscribing their names in the Black Register (the ledger of transgressions) for the sin of disdaining the scripture. Their allotted years are deducted from the Azure Record (the scroll of merits). After death, they are bound and imprisoned in dark dungeons, shackled in the courts of hell. They wander endlessly through the Three Paths of suffering, with no means of escape.

In this present life, some endure all manner of illnesses—scabies, leprosy, abscesses, ulcers, and other grievous afflictions—as karmic retribution, repaying their evil deeds. Sorrow, grief, and torment consume them day and night, their suffering unrelenting. Others suffer violent misfortunes or are struck by calamity. Some are imprisoned in fetters, or meet untimely deaths by their own hands. Some are harmed by poison, or devoured and mauled by tigers, wolves, or venomous serpents. Some are seized by creditors and subjected to harm. Some, while traveling through mountains and forests, encounter brigands who murder them, dismember them, or cast them from cliffs, wounding their bodies and ending their lives. Some are secretly harmed by malevolent spirits and spectral beings. Some perish in fires or are swept away by floods. Some are slain by the sword in war. For the sin of disdaining this scripture, they suffer untimely deaths, cut short in their prime, denied a peaceful end. Such are the karmic consequences, such is the retribution—how can this not be lamentable? How can it not be agonizing?

Grieving their suffering, we also mourn their folly—this is the great compassion of the Heavenly Worthy.

**Resolving Doubts:**
If even a single act of disdain for this scripture incurs boundless retribution, then revering the Dao and believing in the scripture must be a matter of constancy. Through long cultivation, the transformative power of the Dao is perfected, and the efficacy of the scripture is fully realized, bestowing its virtue and manifesting its effects. Yet people, lacking steadfastness, begin with diligence but end in indolence—no wonder sin and karma befall them.

From ancient times to the present, those of inferior capacity abound—many neither uphold nor even hear the scripture, yet suffer misfortunes and calamities. Are they all guilty of disdaining the scripture? The scripture speaks of the Dao; to disbelieve in the Dao is to lack virtue, which is itself the sin of disdaining the scripture. Without virtue, there is sin—this is the sin of disdaining the scripture. To revere the scripture and dissolve sin, one must advance in the Dao and cultivate virtue.

**Chapter Sixteen of the "Manifestations of Efficacy" Continued:**
You should diligently and earnestly follow and practice this without fail, refraining from all perverse thoughts. If all thoughts cease to arise, the myriad conditions instantly subside, and the dust-like evil karma dissolves with the heart. All disasters and demonic influences naturally vanish. The merit of this scripture is inconceivable—it is truly the unparalleled, supreme, and wondrous Dharma, the king of all scriptures, of immense benefit, not to be shown to the unworthy.

The Heavenly Worthy said to the Perfected Ones: Such is the retribution for the sin of disdaining the scripture. You should diligently and earnestly follow and practice this scriptural Dharma without fail, refraining from all perverse thoughts. If all杂念 cease to arise—no good thoughts, no evil thoughts, no thought of no-thought, no thought of no-no-thought—the myriad conditions instantly subside. The five aggregates are emptied, the nine consciousnesses dissolved, the four images transcended, the hundred errors severed, the ten thousand existences voided, and the mind extinguished into utter nothingness. External conditions, internal conditions, karmic conditions, blessed conditions, and the wondrous condition of emptiness—along with attachments to past lives, the present, and future rebirths—are all cut off, their roots and dust fully exhausted. The dust-like evil karma, as countless as the sands of the Ganges, dissolves with the believing heart. All disasters and demonic influences naturally perish. Such is the inconceivable merit of this scripture—truly the unparalleled, supreme, and wondrous Dharma, without equal among the Three Teachings or the ten thousand methods. It is the king of all scriptures, surpassing even the Confucian classics. It is the sovereign of all scriptures across the Three Teachings, of immense benefit, not to be shown to the unworthy. Though the Dao may be learned by all, it must not be transmitted to those who lack faith and reverence—it is not to be shown to the unworthy, nor imparted to those who do not honor the Dao.

**Resolving Doubts:**
Confucians do not instruct those who abandon themselves to despair. Buddhas do not save those without affinity. The Daoist Dharma is most exalted—how could it be forced upon the faithless? Alas, faith is the foundation of advancing in the Dao, and true resolve is the essence of attaining sagehood. Without faith, the scripture cannot be transmitted. One must be utterly sincere and resolute, for this is the root of receiving the scripture and entering sagehood.

**Chapter Seventeen of the "Manifestations of Efficacy" Continued:**
If the gods and humans of the heavens, afflicted by the five decays and four signs, pressed by the cycle of rebirth, can abandon delusive thoughts and uphold this scripture, they will naturally attain celestial blessings, their heavenly fortune secured. They will transcend the Three Realms and abide with the Dao forever. This scripture is most exalted—it must not be recklessly shown to others.

Among the gods and humans, the six heavens of the Desire Realm still indulge in sensual pleasures. The eighteen heavens of the Form Realm abide in form. The four heavens of the Formless Realm delight in the emptiness of meditative absorption. The four Great Brahma heavens know emptiness and nothingness, though they lack desire for pleasure, their tranquil blessings are still finite. Beyond these heavens, the four highest heavens of the Seed People are free from the cycles of formation and dissolution. The heavens below them, though differing in rank, are subject to formation and dissolution.

**The Five Decays and Four Signs:**
The five decays have been explained earlier. The four signs refer to the decline of the marks of non-self, non-person, non-sentient being, and non-lifespan, or the decline of blessed marks and virtuous marks, divided into four phases. Another interpretation holds that the four signs are birth, prime, aging, and death. Yet another defines them as loss of dignity, loss of supernatural powers, loss of joy, and loss of vitality.

**The Cycle of Rebirth Pressing Upon Them:**
The thirty-two heavens of the Desire, Form, Brahma, and Formless Realms vary in duration. When the blessings of their heavenly beings are exhausted, they are reborn among humans, each according to their allotted span—hence the cycle of rebirth.

**Abandoning Delusive Thoughts:**
Delusive thoughts arise from following conditions—whether thoughts of blessings, wisdom, longevity, emptiness, absorption, ascent, or descent. The mind’s endless fabrications, beyond enumeration, are all called delusive thoughts. To abandon them is to cast aside all杂念, leaving no single condition to arise, attaining utter emptiness—this is the relinquishing of illusory thoughts. By abandoning delusive thoughts, one naturally attains celestial blessings, their heavenly fortune further secured, without need for striving.

The realms of Desire, Form, Formless, and Brahma are each their own dwelling, yet from them one may attain liberation—forever free from the cycle of rebirth, immune to decay and dissolution, achieving great steadfastness beyond the wheel of existence. The body transcends the Three Realms. By rising above the Desire Realm, one ascends through the Form Realm, the Formless Realm, and the Brahma Realm, surpassing the worlds of heaven, earth, and humanity, emerging from the three conditioned states to soar into the highest, mysterious celestial bounds. Abiding with the Dao in perpetuity, merging with the true Dao, enduring through infinite kalpas.

The effects of the *Treasure Sutra of Heavenly Beings* may thus be known to the world. Take heed.

**Chapter Eighteen: Verification of Efficacy**
This scripture is unmatched, unsurpassed—possessing great majesty and power, a divine incantation of profound efficacy. It can cause all withered stalks to sprout branches and leaves, to blossom with flowers and bear fruit in lush abundance. It dispels the most grievous suffering of sentient beings and grants longevity to those of short lifespan. The merits of this scripture are inconceivable, its praises beyond exhaustion.

Those who disbelieve in this scripture incur karmic consequences as described; those who trust in it, whether heavenly beings or humans, witness its effects as proclaimed. This scripture stands without equal—all other sutras and dharmas fall beneath it, unrivaled in kind, unmatched in excellence. No other teaching can compare; none surpass its benefits.

It is of great majesty and power, for it both dispels evil and upholds the true. A teaching that cannot encompass both is not called majestic. This scripture eradicates evil and dissolves transgressions, supports the upright and perfects the real—such divine and wondrous virtue, its grace and might immeasurable, is thus called majesty and power.

A divine incantation of the highest order, as explained before: it can cause all withered stalks to sprout branches and leaves, to flourish with flowers and fruit. All grasses, trees, rare blossoms, exotic herbs, auspicious fungi, and sacred fragrances—any living thing that fails to thrive, any that withers or decays—this scripture restores to life, reviving branches, leaves, blossoms, and fruit in luxuriant abundance.

It dispels the most grievous suffering of sentient beings. This scripture dissolves karmic obstructions, benefiting life; it uproots transgressions, aiding in death. It averts demonic calamities, safeguarding joy. Through eons of enmity and desire, lifelong afflictions, the heaviest of sorrows, and burdens accumulated over lifetimes—this scripture severs their very roots.

It grants longevity to those of short lifespan. Among mortals, seventy years is rare. This scripture preserves life and extends years, allowing those who perish young or are fated for brief lives to add to their allotted span and attain longevity. Some may ask: Yan Hui, a near-sage, died at thirty—did his virtue not benefit him? How then can this scripture extend life? For one who dies yet does not perish is truly long-lived. Having attained the Dao in clarity, Yan Hui did not die. The scripture’s power to prolong short lives enables beings to perceive the Dao and thus transcend death without perishing.

The merits of this scripture are inconceivable, its praises beyond exhaustion.

Alas! Minister Kou’s loyalty and righteousness turned withered bamboo into a forest; the Tian clan’s filial piety and fraternal love revived withered thorns. When sages reign above, every household merits enfeoffment, and death and life become joyous. In the fullness of the Great Dao, no virtue or power remains unattained.

Those who contemplate this scripture, take heed—supreme merit and power await. **Verifications, Chapter Nineteen**

Without having planted the roots of the Dao in past lives or widely sown the fields of blessings, one may not even hear the scripture’s name, let alone behold its text. The merits of this scripture are indeed inexhaustible.

If one has not planted the roots of the Dao—*roots* being the foundation—then, as the noble person attends to the root, once the root is established, the Dao arises. Hence, it is called the *root of the Dao*. (One version mistakenly writes *seed of the Dao*.) *Su* means "long-standing" or "preparatory"; *zhi* means "to establish," "to plant," or "to sow." The *root of the Dao* refers to the gradual progression of cultivation, the foundation for entering the Dao. The *Daoist Scriptures* state: "The Great Dao has no name." Through refined cultivation, it is perfected. Confucius said: "Study the lower to reach the higher." The Buddhist scriptures say: "Through hearing, reflection, and cultivation, one enters samādhi." All three teachings esteem learning as the foundation for attaining the Dao.

Yet though the Dao may be suddenly realized, it must be gradually cultivated—truly, it cannot be mastered in a single step. Without prior practice in past or present lives, without first planting the roots of the Dao and widely sowing the fields of blessings—*fields of blessings* being where merit accumulates with effect, like sowing a field—then:

**External deeds** include repairing palaces, temples, hermitages, ancestral halls, shrines, pagodas, pavilions, communal halls, bridges, roads; administering medicine and treating illness; aiding the state and saving people; issuing talismans and performing rituals; praying for offspring; benefiting beings; dispelling evil; averting calamity; relieving suffering; and rescuing the poor.

**Internal practices** include compassion, joyful giving, forbearance, harmonious radiance, universal and impartial generosity, and steadfast perfection.

**Coarse actions** include donating wealth, clothing, and food.

**Secret practices** include contemplating the Dao and meditating on the True, cutting off desire to nurture spirit, accumulating essence and storing qi.

To ascend to sagehood and transcend the mundane—such methods are manifold. The boundless fields of blessings must all be widely sown.

Those who cultivate worldly fields may face droughts, floods, or human mismanagement, resulting in poor harvests. But those who cultivate the fields of blessings—whether in small or great measure—shall reap small or great rewards. The hidden virtues are manifold; even if one remains unaware or their enumeration is inexhaustible, their merit is boundless. Should one fail to cultivate these fields of blessings, they may not even hear the name of this scripture. Without virtue or fortune, the scripture’s name remains unheard.

As the scripture states, the true Dharma is rarely encountered—this is what it means. How much more so to behold this scripture itself? To see this true scripture is to possess the Dao-eye. Without virtue or fortune, its name goes unheard; how then could one hope to see it?

Each person’s capacity differs, as does their reception. Some possess fortune but lack virtue—wealthy and noble yet foolish. Others possess virtue but lack fortune—poor yet sagely. When virtue and fortune flourish together, one attains rank, longevity, and renown. Should one encounter the true Dharma and gain insight into liberation, it is not through mere kalpas of cultivation or boundless merit that such a state is reached.

At a gathering of a million buddhas, the true Dharma was entrusted to Mahākāśyapa. Among Confucius’ three thousand disciples, the unifying principle was passed to Zeng Shen. In the Daoist tradition, though its followers number in the millions, only Wenshi inherited the heart of the Most High.

Let the reader take heed.

**Chapter Twenty: Verification**

This scripture adheres to the true standards of the Three Caverns. It is transmitted but once in eighty thousand kalpas—a treasure cherished by the perfected sages of the Pure Capital, secreted within the Golden Towers of the Jade Capital. Most wondrous and subtle, it is rarely encountered. Only those with the bone of immortality—destined to become true immortals of the Nine Heavens—may chance upon this text. Having met with this opportunity, they shall later present gold and treasures to their master, swear oaths, and receive it. Only then, when the time is ripe and fate aligns, may it be transmitted. It must not be lightly disclosed; revere it, guard it well. This scripture is not to be casually passed on.

It adheres to the true standards of the Three Caverns: the Cavern of Truth, the Cavern of Mystery, and the Cavern of Spirit—these three ranks are recorded in the celestial statutes and golden precepts. The "true standards" are the natural principles. "Eighty thousand kalpas" refers to the cycles of cosmic renewal spanning eighty thousand great kalpas. One tradition holds that human life begins at *yin* and ends at *you*, with eight periods totaling 86,400 years—roughly eighty thousand years. Each cycle of growth, harvest, and renewal is called a kalpa. "Sand" signifies innumerable grains. The transmission of this scripture once in eighty thousand kalpas marks the turning of the great Dharma-wheel—an exceedingly rare occurrence. The earlier explanation is nearly correct.

This is a treasure of the perfected sages of the Pure Capital of Supreme Clarity, secreted within the Golden Towers of the Jade Capital—not the abode of the Jade Emperor, but the repository of the *Tai Xuan Bao Zang Di Jing*. Most wondrous and subtle, its praises extol the difficulty of encountering it. Only those with the bone of immortality—those who have cultivated in past lives and possess the destiny of transcendence—may become true immortals of the Nine Heavens. Having met with this text and this karmic connection, they shall later present gold and treasures to their master, swear oaths, and receive it. This is not merely the offering of worldly gold and treasures, but the pledge of a heart utterly sincere and selfless, unwavering for all kalpas—a vow sworn before heaven and earth, as pure as the midday sun, untarnished by even a speck of dust. Such is true, indestructible gold.

Only then, when the moment arrives and fate aligns, may it be transmitted. It must not be lightly disclosed. To "disclose" is to leak; the scripture’s essence must not be carelessly revealed, nor shared with the unworthy. Revere it, guard it well. Alas! Knowing the weight of this scripture, all should cultivate good karma as the foundation for receiving it. Once received, it must never be treated lightly. Treasure it, strive for it.

**Chapter Twenty-One: Verification (continued)**

Then the Heavenly Worthy expounded this meaning again and spoke in verse:

*Were the waters of rivers and streams*
*To bear lotuses in their waves,*
*Or the feathers of kind crows turn white—*
*This scripture would still be hard to meet.*

*Were turtles to grow fur,*
*Fine enough for robes and garments,*
*Or the moon at night melt ice—*
*This scripture would still be hard to meet.*

*Were the legs of gnats and midges*
*Strong enough to build bridges,*
*To bear all manner of weight—*
*This scripture would still be hard to meet.*

*Were fledgling sparrows*
*Able to carry great mountains,*
*And cast them to distant lands—*
*This scripture would still be hard to meet.*

*Were a single leaf of a boat*
*To bear the weight of Kunlun,*
*And ferry it across the sea—*
*This scripture would still be hard to meet.*

*Were leeches to grow tusks,*
*As large as an elephant’s,*
*From their very mouths—*
*This scripture would still be hard to meet.*

*Were the leaves of mugwort*
*To cover boundless realms,*
*And shade the Kunlun peaks—*
*This scripture would still be hard to meet.*

*Were crows and owls*
*To nest together in one tree,*
*Feeding and nurturing one another—*
*This scripture would still be hard to meet.*

*Were rabbits to grow horns,*
*Fit for ladders to the heavens,*
*To climb to the very summit—*
*This scripture would still be hard to meet.*

*Were mice to climb*
*Those rabbit-horn ladders,*
*And devour the moon in the sky—*
*This scripture would still be hard to meet.*

*Were donkeys’ lips*
*To turn the hue of *pingpo* fruit,*
*And sing and dance as well—*
*This scripture would still be hard to meet.*

*Were flies and insects*
*To drink barrels of wine,*
*And grow drunk and lost—*
*This scripture would still be hard to meet.*

Then the Heavenly Worthy of Primordial Beginning expounded again the merit of the scripture and spoke in verse:

*Were the waters of rivers and streams*
*To bear lotuses in their waves...*

The rivers run deep, their currents vast and waves towering—yet they cannot bear lotuses. The feathers of kind crows are naturally black—yet they cannot turn white. To speak of white crows or lotus-bearing rivers is to describe the impossible, and thus to illustrate the extreme rarity of encountering this scripture.

Turtles are naturally without fur, and the moon is inherently the Great Yin. To speak of gathering turtle fur for clothing or melting ice with the night moon is but to describe the utterly nonexistent—thus illustrating the extreme rarity of encountering this scripture. Moreover, to say that the legs of mosquitoes and midges could construct bridges to bear heavy loads; that fledglings with yellow beaks could carry great mountains and cast them into other realms; that a small boat could ferry Mount Kunlun across the vast sea; or that leeches could grow the tusks of elephants—leeches being but tiny water insects—these too are impossibilities. Or that the leaves of mugwort and reeds could cover boundless worlds and shade Mount Kunlun; that crows and owls, both ill-omened birds, could nest together and feed one another in filial reciprocity; that rabbit horns could serve as ladders to ascend to heaven; that a shrew could climb such a ladder to reach the heavens and devour the moon; that donkeys’ lips could turn the hue of *pingpo* fruit and sing and dance; or that flies and insects could drink barrels of wine and grow drunk and lost—all these are things that must never exist, principles that have never been. Yet they are recounted in detail to show how difficult it is to encounter this scripture.

The Great Dao is possessed by all, and the true scripture is complete in each. Even the foolish and the dull-witted have their correct nature and may all be transformed and delivered—why then compare the rarity of this scripture to such impossibilities? Could it truly never be transmitted? The answer lies in the hierarchy of teachings: this scripture is the most exalted. Those who transmit it may not embody the truth of the Primordial Beginning, and those who receive it may not grasp the secrets of the Jade Emperor. With obstructions and biases, the full scripture remains obscure. Some may never hear or see it; others may hear or see it in vain, their understanding of its merit and efficacy left vague. Without attaining its full benefit, one must indeed acknowledge its rarity.

Yet the difficulty of difficulty need not be feared. With utmost sincerity and reverence, the merit of the scripture will naturally be met.

**Chapter 22: Manifestations of Response (Continued)**
Then the Heavenly Worthy, having expounded the verse, addressed the four assemblies: Should there be one in the world who upholds this scripture, they are called a *Body of Merit and Virtue*, for all sentient beings are sheltered beneath their shade. They are called a *Body of Spiritual Penetration*, for all auspicious signs gather in their presence. They are called a *Body of Purity*, for no evil karma can assail them.

Then the Heavenly Worthy of Primordial Beginning, having expounded the verse, addressed the four assemblies. *Pu* means "all-pervading"; the *four assemblies* have been explained previously.

Should there be one in the world who upholds this scripture, they are called a *Body of Merit and Virtue*. That which benefits others is *merit*; that which enriches others is *virtue*. To benefit all beings without exception is to embody the wondrous merit and mysterious virtue of the profound Dao. All sentient beings—whether in the realms of the moving or the growing, all that has life, even earth, wood, metal, and stone, so long as they possess essence and spirit—are sheltered beneath their shade. The sages of the Three Teachings universally ferry and eternally benefit all. *Yin* means "to cover." The upholder of the scripture benefits self and others, humans and things, the knowing and the unknowing, bestowing universal grace—all are enveloped in their compassionate shade.

They are called a *Body of Spiritual Penetration*. Though humans are the most spiritually endowed, they possess no spiritual penetration. The wondrous Dharma within this scripture is supremely profound and divine, unobstructedly transcendent—thus the upholder of this true scripture is called a *Body of Spiritual Penetration*. All auspicious signs gather in their presence. The sages of the Three Teachings are eternally perfected and indestructible. *Xian* means "all"; *zhen* means "to arrive"; *ji* means "to assemble." The upholder of the scripture, in their own conduct, is harmonious and auspicious; in their dealings with others, they are loving and impartial; in their governance of the world and the state, they are never misplaced. The blessings they attain are manifold, with a thousand auspicious signs converging. Heaven does not withhold the Dao; Earth does not hoard its treasures. People submit to their transformation; things respond to their numinous power—all auspicious signs gather in their presence.

They are called a *Body of Purity*. That which is unstained is *pure*; that which is unburdened is *clear*. The upholder of the scripture quiets all entanglements, leaving not a single flaw—thus no evil can assail them. *Qin* means "to encroach and harm." Whether self-inflicted karma, heavenly calamities, past transgressions, or present faults; whether the misfortunes of ancestors or the obstructions of the times; whether the afflictions of fate, the clashes of stellar positions, the assaults of demons and ghosts, the slander of people, or the malevolence of things—all such evil karma is transformed from inauspicious to auspicious, unable to encroach or harm. The person of the Dao encompasses Heaven and Earth, nurturing all beings. Their blessings and felicity are everlasting, and Heaven’s protection never wanes. The virtuous are aided by Heaven, free from disaster and harm—truly a *Body of Merit, Spiritual Penetration, and Purity*. Those who merely possess a body but lack merit, spiritual penetration, and purity do not truly uphold the scripture. Strive on, and one may verify its efficacy for oneself.

**Chapter 23: Manifestations of Response (Continued)**
They are called a *Body of Majestic Virtue*, for neither heavenly demons nor heterodox paths can subdue them. They are called a *Body Without Equal*, for the Supreme Emperor extols them from afar, and all spirits revere them.

The upholder of this scripture is not only called a *Body of Purity* but also a *Body of Majestic Virtue* (as explained previously). *Heavenly demons and heterodox paths* include the demon kings of the heavens, demonic men and women, dark and shadowy demons, asuras, *gusiluo*, *chi*, *mei*, *wangliang*, spirits, deviants, specters, and malevolent forces—along with the deceptions of worldly people, the heresies of external paths, and all manner of inauspicious, seductive, and aberrant influences. *Cannot subdue*: *She* means "to summon, tame, and subjugate." When demons and heterodox forces invade, people are deluded and submit unknowingly—this is called *she*. For example, in the Buddhist tradition, Ānanda was ensnared by the courtesan Matangi and nearly violated his precepts. Yet the upholder of this scripture, having awakened to the seal of the mind, is selfless and non-attached, silently upholding the true scripture. Their Dharma power is complete, their Dao lofty, and their virtue weighty—majestic and radiant beyond measure. Ghosts and demons lose their courage; spirits and specters vanish without form. How could any evil or demon dare to assail the upright? Thus, *heavenly demons and heterodox paths cannot subdue them*. Ānanda, though upholding the precepts, was moved by a single thought toward Matangi—having not yet awakened to the essence of the mind and lacking the scripture’s guidance, he was ensnared by her.

Were it not for the power of the Buddha’s *Śūraṅgama Sūtra*, how could one preserve their entire being? This *August Scripture* is the sovereign among all scriptures, and the majestic virtue of its upholders is ten thousand times greater than that of other scriptures. Those who uphold this scripture are not merely called *majestic-virtue bodies*; they are called *bodies beyond compare*—bodies without equal, without peer, without counterpart. Truly so.

The upholders of this scripture stand supreme, with none to rival them. As the scriptural commentary states: *"Nothing compares to the Buddha."* The Confucian texts say: *"The Master’s unattainability is like the heavens—one cannot ascend by steps."* The distant chant of the Heavenly Emperors—here, *Heavenly Emperors* does not refer to the Jade Emperor but to the Emperors of all heavens—*distant* meaning far, *chant* signifying the celestial proclamation praising those who advance in the Dao. Some interpret *chant* as *ballad*, implying song, but this is contextually unsound and should not be followed. For when a boy is born in the world, the myriad spirits chant in reverence; when a girl is born, the myriad spirits chant in service. The Heavenly Emperors cherish life, and all heavens rejoice. The person of the Dao, whose sincerity moves heaven and earth and stirs ghosts and spirits, harmonizes their virtue with heaven—thus do the Heavenly Emperors celebrate. Yet these Emperors are most exalted, unlike other spirits; from their celestial palaces, they merely hear the distant celestial tones in joy, not the words of song. Otherwise, why would this text first speak of the *distant chant of the Heavenly Emperors* and then of the *reverence of the myriad spirits*? The *myriad spirits*—not one but many—encompass all spirits, high and low, who all revere the sacred canon. When they encounter a true upholder of the scripture, a person of the Dao, none fail to show respect.

Human nature is inherently firm, luminous, centered, and harmonious, standing shoulder to shoulder with the Primordial Beginning, inherently possessing great majestic virtue beyond compare. Yet if one cannot restrain emotions and desires, evil enters, and the spirits do not revere them. Alas! Zixia was a worthy man, yet upon seeing splendor and beauty, he was delighted—had he not received the true teaching of the Master, how could he have become a lofty disciple? If one can remain single-mindedly true without pretense, they shall forever attain the Dao.

**Verification Chapter, Continuation of the Twenty-Fourth Section**

Those who uphold this scripture are called *indestructible bodies*, for great calamities and dire perils cannot harm them. Those who uphold this scripture are called *Dao-treasury bodies*, for when they speak, ghosts and spirits look up in reverence.

Those who uphold this scripture are not merely called *bodies beyond compare*; they are called *indestructible bodies*. The lives of worldly people are impermanent, their cycles of rebirth unknowable. Some achieve minor merits and minor virtues, their blessings and destinies limited; some cultivate minor roots and minor vehicles, their fruition constrained; some are enlightened yet not fully clear, easily obscured and deceived; some practice yet do not perfect, easily interrupted and severed; some save others and relieve suffering, yet their capacity is quickly exhausted. Their bodies are like duckweed or candle flames, unable to withstand wind and waves—such is not indestructibility. The true upholder of the scripture, whose mind never regresses, is called an *indestructible body*; thus, great calamities and dire perils cannot harm them. Whether in minor, medium, or great kalpas—through cosmic cycles, worldly transformations, the destruction and renewal of the Three Realms, the annihilation of all beings, the collapse of earth and sky, the collision of the four wheels, the mutual conquest of the five phases, the disasters of water, fire, and wind that ravage the world, the dissolution of all phenomena, the exhaustion of all dharmas, the fruitions of all vehicles, the four dhyāna heavens, the three ranks and nine grades, the officials, spirits, generals, and clerks—all are swept into the cycle of rebirth, facing the tribulation of recreation. Yet the true upholder of the scripture attains great indestructibility, transcending kalpas and cycles, never regressing. Whether in the calamities of cosmic cycles, the disasters of past karma, or unforeseen great perils, not even a hair’s breadth can encroach upon them. They remain steadfast through constancy and change, unwavering for aeons—unharmed.

Those who uphold this scripture are not merely called *indestructible bodies*; they are called *Dao-treasury bodies*. *Treasury* means to store, to treasure. The Dao encompasses all, and there is nothing it does not perfect. The *Dao-treasury* contains all without omission, treasuring all without exception. Though the human body is limited, the nature is boundless. Yet if one does not fully realize the Dao, they do not become a *Dao-body*. The true upholder of the scripture, profound as the abyss and vast as the sea, contains within their Dharma-treasury infinite worlds—thus are they called *Dao-treasury bodies*. When they speak, ghosts and spirits look up in reverence. *Answering and expounding* is called *speech*; *self-expression* is called *words*.

People have said: if ghosts and spirits do not look up in reverence, it is because words lack sincerity—mere verbal transgressions may yet be forgiven, but how can one move humans and gods, ghosts and spirits?

The true upholder of the scripture speaks with perfect trustworthiness, their words emerging with order, neither hasty nor concealed, neither failing others nor failing speech. A single word may continue the pulse of a thousand truths; a single word may open the transmission of ten thousand generations; a single word may rescue beings from drowning; a single utterance may shatter the barrier of life and death; a single word may exhaust the Dao of heaven and earth; a single phrase may encompass the mysteries of the ten thousand dharmas; a single word may reveal the conditions of ghosts and spirits; a single utterance may clarify the rise and entanglement of kalpas. A single word may extinguish the tally of sin and merit; a single phrase may unveil the golden elixir.

The true essence of the Three Teachings lies in a single word of this scripture—cherished by all beings, unknown even to ghosts and spirits. Who would not revere and look up in awe? That ghosts and spirits look up in reverence is thus explained.

The great sages of the Three Teachings, unwavering through kalpas, are revered in speech by both heaven and humanity—this confirms the principle of the indestructible *Dao-treasury*. How could a mere upholder of the scripture attain such heights? It is because one gain becomes eternal gain, a truth proven in principle. The fault lies only in not truly upholding the scripture.

What does *true upholding* entail? A heart of fasting as the foundation, transformed and perfected by spirit.

*Collected Commentaries on the August Scripture, Scroll Nine* concludes.
*Collected Commentaries on the August Scripture, Scroll Ten* begins.

**Chapter on Verification, Continuation Twenty-Five**

One who upholds this scripture is called the *Body of Compassion*. The beings of the Six Paths rely upon their goodness.

One who upholds this scripture is not merely called the *Body of the Dao-treasury*, but the *Body of Compassion*. Great benevolence and boundless grace—this is what is meant by *compassion*. Yet people, divided by the distinction between self and other, their minds set on the fleeting and the lasting, not only fail to universally bestow eternal benefit, thus falling short of compassion; even when they grant favors or deliver others, their aid is limited and impermanent—this, too, is not the *Body of Compassion*. Thus, to distribute wealth to others is called *benevolence*; to teach goodness is called *loyalty*. Petty favors and private kindnesses are not universal salvation.

One who truly upholds the scripture is no ordinary body—he is the *Great Body of Compassion*, the *Great Body of Mercy*. How is this known? The beings of the Six Paths rely upon his goodness.

The kinds of the world are exhausted in the Six Paths. The Six Paths contain multitudes, and these multitudes are difficult to fully deliver. The Heavenly Path stands lofty and exalted, its blessings pure and enduring, yet its origins unseen—how can its numbers be counted? Though heaven is high, it still encompasses the Three Realms: desire, form, and formlessness, all subject to arising and decay. Even if their blessings vary in duration, apart from the lords of each heaven, only those who uphold the true Dao endure. As for the myriad heavenly beings and all their kinds, none abide forever; all have beginning and end, and thus are called *sentient beings*.

The Human Path: humanity is the spirit of the ten thousand things. Though they praise transformation and nurture, propagate the great Dao and its teachings, their stations differ in nobility, their forms in male and female, their numbers teeming like forests, beyond reckoning—these, too, are called *sentient beings*.

The Asura Path: the demon kings of heaven, demon subjects, demon women, spirits and deviants, monstrosities and portents—these are the demonic kinds of sentient beings.

The Hell Path: the Five Uninterrupted Hells, the Nine Dark Hells, the Eighteen Great Hells, the Thirty-Six Hells, the Seventy-Two Hells—all their suffering and torment belong to the hellish kinds of sentient beings.

The Hungry Ghost Path: the gloomy depths of Fengdu, the mighty ghost kings, ghost messengers, those with elongated limbs and massive heads, distended bellies and slender throats, who for ten thousand kalpas never hear the sound of broth or water—these are the hungry ghost kinds of sentient beings.

The Animal Path: those born from wombs, eggs, moisture, or transformation; creatures of feather, fur, scale, or shell; all their dull and mindless forms—these are the animal kinds of sentient beings.

All bear the merciful grace of the scripture’s upholder, who lifts them, delivers them, and shelters them. Thus, he secures the blessings of heaven and humanity, resolves the calamities of the human realm, releases those sunk in demonic and hellish realms, and extinguishes the retributions of ghosts and beasts. In one world, ten thousand worlds, or the three-thousand-great-thousand worlds, the beings of the Six Paths, freed from karmic bonds and enriched in blessings, rely upon his goodness. This is called *compassion*.

To speak of the Six Paths is to encompass all kinds; to speak of sentient beings is to encompass all multitudes. All worlds are thus—from the beginningless past to the endless future, all that is beyond thought and measure relies upon the goodness of one who truly upholds the scripture. Truly, this is *great compassion*!

Alas! First deliver oneself, then deliver others. To save the beings of the Six Paths, one must forever uphold the true scripture, perfecting supreme goodness before it is possible.

**Chapter on Verification, Continuation Twenty-Six**

One who upholds this scripture is called the *Great Dao Body*, for wherever he goes, he knows no fear.

One who upholds this scripture is not merely called the *Body of Compassion*, but the *Great Dao Body*. The Dao is originally formless, while the human body has shape. Human nature contains the Dao in its entirety, possessing the principles of heaven and earth and their qi. If one speaks of the *Dao Body*, then all people possess the Dao, yet none fully realize it—thus, the body born of father and mother may simply be called the *human body*. The Four Great Elements disperse, so it may be called the *illusory body*. It remains material and untransformed, so it may be called the *ordinary body*. Living in drunkenness and dreaming, it may be called the *unaware body*. Rushing about from dawn to dusk, content to age and die, it may be called the *karmic body*. Without cultivation or learning, trapped in the cycle of rebirth, it may be called the *sinful body*. Like autumn fragrance, fleeting and brief, it may be called the *sensory body*. Male and female differ in form, old and young in appearance, so it may be called the *impermanent body*. When the great limit arrives, even the six relations cannot be relied upon, so it may be called the *lonely body*. Assailed by wind and rain, heat and cold, it may be called the *suffering body*. Such is the body of one who lacks the Dao.

One who truly upholds the scripture, attaining the Dharma and becoming perfected, is the *Dao Body*—no ordinary body, but the *Great Dao Body*. Wherever he goes, he knows no fear.

*Fear* is alarm and anxiety; *dread* is terror. Only one who upholds the scripture, called the *Great Dao Body*, moves with ease when going out and carries no burden when returning. Whether coming or going, he is pure, liberated, and at peace—always serene. What worry, what fear? Wherever he is, he knows no fear.

**Resolving Doubts:**
The Confucians say: *"Be cautious in what you do not see, fearful in what you do not hear."* Is not caution and fear itself a kind of dread? Moreover, the seven emotions—joy, anger, sorrow, thought, grief, terror, and shock—reside in every person. Yet it is said that wherever one goes, he knows no fear—does this not contradict caution and fear?

It is not so. Caution and fear restrain human desire to preserve the true Dao. The seven emotions demonstrate the full functioning of human nature, guiding people to subdue their passions and return to their nature. But if the heart of dread arises from inner instability, giving rise to external calamity, and one is seized by terror, this is not the proper way. Thus, the Master said: *"Look within and find no fault—what worry, what fear?"* Wherever he goes, he knows no fear, for body and Dao are one, and all he encounters is peace. What fear could there be?

The Confucians also say: *"Seeing the great, the heart is at peace."* This and the above illuminate each other. For one who upholds the scripture, fearlessness in coming and going is natural—not forced.

Forced restraint, like Gaozi’s "unmoving heart," ultimately fails to perceive the Dao. Spontaneity, however—Confucius’s freedom from doubt and following the heart, Mencius’s "unmoving heart"—all align with the meaning of this scripture. How excellent are the teachings of both traditions on the study of the heart: *"Thusness, naturalness, majestic immovability"*—the way of spiritual transformation is fully contained therein. If one clings through effort, no spontaneous transformation occurs, and the practice cannot endure. Seeking quick results, even without fear, still does not accord with the scripture’s meaning.

**Verification Chapter, Continuation of Chapter 27**
One who upholds this scripture is called the "Good Physician of the Body," for they skillfully practice the wondrous Dharma to bring peace and joy to others. One who upholds this scripture is not only called the "Body of the Great Dao" but also the "Good Physician of the Body." *Yi* (physician) means "fitting." Medicine fits the illness, removing its obstructions. Worldly physicians examine forms and discern principles, curing people’s ailments and rescuing them from distress. With medicine, they dispel sickness and sustain life, earning names like "divine," "sagely," "masterful," or "skillful."

The true upholder of this scripture is no worldly physician, nor any ordinary healer, nor one who speaks of elixirs from distant shores. Silently transmitting the golden needle, they are called the "Good Physician of the Body." Skillfully practicing the wondrous Dharma, they address the myriad conditions of human life: countless beings with tangled qi and temperament, qi losing its balance, bearing illnesses from birth, visible ailments, invisible afflictions, personal suffering, familial distress, the troubles of a nation or the world, the woes of an age, or the karmic burdens of countless kalpas. Sorrows and entanglements follow endlessly—beyond the correction of parents, the transformation of sages, or the restraint of royal law; incurable by medicine, unsavable by any remedy.

Yet the upholder of this scripture does not resort to the strange or the coercive. With wondrous application, fitting and apt, the good Dharma is neither harsh nor grating, neither contrary to one’s heart nor burdensome to the body. It does not force what is difficult nor indulge through deception. Skillfully practicing the wondrous Dharma to bring peace and joy, with the first application of this wondrous Dharma, the deaf regain hearing; with the second, the blind see clearly; with the third, the mute speak.

With the fourth application, the lame and chronically ill rise and walk; with the fifth, long-standing ailments and stubborn diseases instantly restore the body; with the sixth, white hair returns to black, and fallen teeth regrow; with the seventh, the old regain vigor, and the young grow stronger; with the eighth, women conceive, and the fetuses of birds and beasts—whether already born or yet unborn—all come to full formation; with the ninth, the earth’s hidden treasures are revealed, and gold and jade emerge into view; with the tenth, withered bones revive, all rising as whole persons.

It dissolves all disasters and delivers from all calamities. Demonic afflictions and karmic debts never again encroach; nine generations of ancestors and six relations—whether from past kalpas or yet to come—find peace in self and others, and the imprisoned in the unseen realms are freed from bondage. Wealth abounds and the body grows light. Not only does it raise those sunk in chronic illness, but it also transforms and dispels what has not yet taken form. Truly, without medicine there is joy, and life is preserved to extend one’s years—yet this is no mere single remedy or technique to bring ease to one body. The Celestial Physician is most excellent, the compassionate father of all beings, the wondrous Dharma that brings peace and joy.

This scripture is the great Dao of transcendence, the marvelous formula for refining the world. It is inherent in all, yet people, lost in delusion, fail to awaken. Those who uphold this true scripture with attainment should aid the world. Why, in this latter age, do selfish thoughts arise? Having gained but a little, they show no compassion to others. Like the people of Qin and Yue, each indifferent to the other’s plight, they neither teach the world at first nor find joy in the end. Their brief enlightenment is soon obscured, and they cannot achieve the Dao—how lamentable!

Alas, to know the scripture yet not deliver the world is wrong. Yet to feign knowledge, delight in being a teacher, and recklessly transmit and initiate others is an even greater error. Only by eliminating these two faults and striving to fully understand this scripture can one universally deliver beings.

**Chapter on Verification, Continued (28)**
The upholder of this scripture is called the Luminous Body, for they are constantly embraced by the Imperial Light.

The upholder of this scripture is not merely a good physician of the body but is named the Luminous Body. Human nature is inherently empty and luminous, illuminating all without obstruction. Yet when qi is constrained by things and obscured by delusion, its brightness is dimmed. The true upholder of this scripture, with nature and heaven radiant, opens the ground of the heart. Light shines constantly, the root of life fuses with essence, like the cloudless vastness of the great void, like the sun and moon alternating in brilliance. Vast and boundless, it flows abundantly.

Truly, the Luminous Body is no defiled or darkened one. They are constantly embraced by the Imperial Light. The *Emperor* is the Jade Emperor. The Imperial Light shines universally, reaching all without exception. Yet people, being base and defiled, find it difficult to receive. Now, the upholder of this scripture treads the light of heaven, their cinnabar radiance illuminating the golden courts. When the cosmos is at peace, the light of heaven emerges. The August Emperor’s presence is manifest, its brilliance suffusing the mysterious void. The upholder of the scripture, looking up, receives it; great harmony and luminous clarity prevail. Embraced by the Imperial Light, the upholder is protected. Having received its transformation, they forever preserve constant purity. Light intermingles and reflects upon the upholder of the scripture.

Their faith aligns with heaven’s virtue, their brightness merges with that of the sun and moon. It is ever heard that the Dao of heaven descends to aid and illumine, inherently impartial. People receive heaven’s bright mandate, inherently great and luminous. Yet their nature is originally unstained, and the heart treasures emptiness and clarity. People, indulging in desires and pleasures, cannot keep the spirit-terrace free of things, like the sun and moon eclipsed, like clouds and mist obscuring the sky. If one does not adorn their faults, cut off desires, and return to principle, the momentary darkness will swiftly clear. Four directions open, eight reaches connect, and the bright grow brighter. Is there a time when the light of heaven does not shine, when the Imperial Light does not descend? Never has such a thing occurred.

Yet people bring ruin upon themselves, abandoning their true nature. Their thoughts swarm ceaselessly, active by day and resting by night, their minds never still for even a moment. What they do is already deluded, and their thoughts are further unclear, opposing heaven. Thus, they obscure the true scripture. A fleeting moment of awareness gives rise to karma. The retribution for sins is severe, and the cycles of suffering are not light. If one cannot swiftly reform and express remorse through words, then heaven cannot be deceived, nor spirits be fooled. People see themselves as if their lungs and liver were exposed—where is this nature that endures such self-deception?

Thus, the mandate of heaven’s protection and the Imperial Light’s auspicious embrace must belong to those who truly uphold the scripture, cultivate the Dao, and achieve transcendence. The true upholder of the scripture is named the Luminous Body, embraced by the Imperial Light.

Alas, all people are one, and their bright virtue is the same. Why do they not uphold the scripture and fulfill the Dao, thereby meeting the heart of the August Emperor?

**Chapter on Verification, Continued (29)**
The upholder of this scripture is called the Self-So Body, for their spirit can roam the wondrous realms of the celestial palaces.

The merits of upholding this scripture cannot be fully described. The upholder of this scripture is not only named the Luminous Body but also the Self-So Body. The three disasters, four killings, five phases, six harms, seven injuries, eight difficulties, nine stellar calamities, and ten evil karmas—all entanglements are severed, and the roots of karma are forever extinguished. They realize the Three Origins, empty the Five Aggregates, depart from the Four Marks, cut off the hundred wrongs, eliminate the ten thousand existences, transcend kalpas, and attain true liberation as the great Self-So Body.

Their spirit can roam the wondrous realms of the celestial palaces. The upholder of this scripture, with mysterious powers at will, attains self-mastery of the body. In all celestial palaces and every wondrous realm—whether the azure paths and jade steps, the coiling dragon walls and painted screens, the jade capital and golden towers, the purple courts and yellow chambers, the jasper towers and jade halls, the Jasper Gardens and Jasper Pools, the Mysterious Capital’s upper realms, the secret chambers and cinnabar terraces, the treasure groves and fragrant paths, the vermilion palaces of the spirit chambers, the Dharma bridges and brocade railings, the bright halls and ochre courts—or the jade grasses and gem flowers, ice peaches and snow plums, intersecting pears and flame dates, cinnabar feasts and jasper nectars, the radiance of the Dao and mysterious auspices, the rosy clouds and propitious vapors, the divine chariots and Dharma carriages, the Dharma treasures and celestial beings, the various marvelous flowers, and the contemplative journeys of the spirit—all surpass what ordinary people can attain.

The merits of upholding this scripture cannot be fully described; this merely hints at their vastness. The various merits of upholding this scripture benefit all beings—whether manifest or hidden, living or dead, past or present. Demons are subdued, the Imperial Light descends, and the spirit roams the celestial realms. Even so, these words do not exhaust its merits, for they are truly boundless.

How then does the recitation of scripture by the sagely compare? Alas, the power of scripture is supreme. One must hold it with utmost sincerity and perpetuity.

**Chapter Thirty-One: Verifications Continued**
Moreover, when Daoist practitioners or aspirants establish an altar to recite the scripture, donning fresh and pure garments, at the stillness after midnight—when the true forms of the *kun-kun* characters are concealed in darkness and silence—they should seclude themselves in a pristine chamber, strike their teeth nine times, face east in upright posture, and chant this scripture. At that moment, the Celestial Perfected attend their seats, the Jade Consorts sweep the ritual mats, the myriad spirits array in ranks, the hosts of heavens descend to the altar, the Three Realms stand guard, and the Five Emperors preside over the reception. Those who uphold this scripture become embodiments of twelve virtues, their compassion and salvation boundless.

Furthermore, regarding "Daoist practitioners or aspirants": *Daoists* is the general term for those who cultivate the Dao and enter the mysterious. *Aspirants* refers to those of resolve—not to be mistaken for *Perfected Beings*, for only those who attain the Dao and achieve transcendence may be called *Perfected*. Some, upon reading passages about the Celestial Perfected, Jade Consorts, myriad spirits, hosts of heavens, Three Realms, and Five Emperors attending and receiving, assume the text refers to *Perfected Beings*. Yet they fail to recognize that this scripture is an imperial decree, and wherever it resides, the gods provide protection. Even if the reciter is a commoner, an elderly person, or a child, the gods revere them as they would the Emperor. Sincerity in upholding the scripture yields boundless blessings, while negligence invites immediate misfortune. The gods attend and receive out of respect for the true scripture, not necessarily for *Perfected Beings*. Moreover, establishing an altar, donning fresh garments, reciting at midnight in a quiet chamber, striking the teeth nine times, and sitting facing east are standard practices for students reciting scripture—not criteria for *Perfected Beings*. Thus, it is clear the text refers to *aspirants*.

When such aspirants, in accordance with the ritual, purify themselves and recite the scripture, at that very moment: the Celestial Perfected attend their seats, the Perfected of all heavens descend to the sacred table. The Jade Consorts sweep the ritual mats—*sweeping* here denotes reverent cleansing, not repulsion, signifying solemn respect. The *mats* are the ritual seats. The myriad spirits array in ranks: just as earthly emperors command their ministers to assemble, this scripture, being the secret words of the Jade Emperor and the decrees of the Primordial Beginning, elevates the reciter to the status of one proclaiming an imperial edict, prompting all spirits to array in attendance. The hosts of heavens descend to the altar: all the Perfected Emperors of the heavens arrive at the sacred table. The Three Realms stand guard: the Three Realms—Heaven, Earth, and the Water Realms—with their divine generals and officials, array to protect. Some say the Three Realms refer to Heaven, Earth, and Humanity, but in the stillness of midnight within a secluded chamber, no humans are present; thus, it undoubtedly refers to the Three Realms of Heaven, Earth, and Water. Others suggest the Three Realms of Desire, Form, and Formlessness, but this is incorrect. The Five Emperors preside over the reception: the Five Emperors are the Five Elders of the Five Directions, the Five Numinous Lords. Some say they are the August Emperors of the Five Peaks. Given the prior mention of the myriad spirits and Three Realms, which include the Five Elders, here it should refer to the myriad spirits of the Five Peaks. Within the Three Realms, the Five Peaks are present, so the Five Elders are appropriate. The scripture contains the Five Elders’ inquiries, and thus, at its opening, the Five Elders preside over the reception of the Dao’s Ancestor. *Preside* means to govern or receive; *receive* means to welcome, embodying the reverence of the Empty Sovereign. Some say *preside over the reception* refers to the spirits of the scripture’s upholder ascending to the mysterious, which is also plausible.

Observing the devotion of scripture recitation, the gods provide full protection; without sincerity, the gods will surely respond in kind. Those who encounter this scripture should hold it with solemn reverence, while those who have not should earnestly seek the opportunity. Be as solemn as if the gods were present, yet do not seek visible effects. Without thought or action, silently uphold the true scripture, and the spirits within your body will forever preserve the mysterious and true. Strive on without negligence.

**Chapter Thirty-Two: Verifications Continued**
Moreover, if one can close their eyes in tranquil contemplation, visualizing the scripture, they will unknowingly find themselves amidst clouds of three colors. Suddenly, their body radiates brilliant light, ascending to the celestial palaces, where the Perfected descend to welcome them. Whatever they request is granted, and elixirs, wondrous treasures, and all desires are attained at will. One should follow the rituals and practice with a devoted heart.

Not only does sincere recitation of the scripture earn the protection of the gods, but one can also close their eyes in tranquil contemplation to reflect on the harms of the Five Aggregates—with form being the foremost. The eyes are the root of form’s disturbance to the mind. Upholding the true scripture requires bodily and mental purity; closing the eyes in stillness naturally stabilizes the mind. Yet the Dao’s profundities cannot be entered without contemplation, while reckless thoughts disrupt the true. Closing the eyes in silent sitting, free from desires, prevents the mind’s chaos, allowing quiet reflection to penetrate the subtle mechanisms—this is the essence of scripture recitation.

Now, regarding "silent sitting": some advise half-closing the eyes, not fully shutting them, to avoid descending into a "ghostly den"—a state where one is unaware of stray thoughts from the dark realms. If the mind is pure, what harm is there in closing the eyes? Yet, as people’s resolve is often weak, closing the eyes may breed doubt or invite drowsiness, hence the advice to keep them half-open to avoid the demon of sleep. If one can forget both good and evil, even the absence of absence, the true Dao will naturally enter—what harm is there in closing the eyes? The "half-closed" teaching is a precaution by the mysterious masters against the demonic delusions of the uncommitted.

Moreover, the meaning of "half-closing the eyes" is to refrain from external observation while maintaining inner illumination. The mind remains alert, its light undimmed, akin to the "drooping curtain" of governance: though not directly observing, one remains fully aware of all matters. This is the subtle mechanism of Dao cultivation, hence its mention alongside the discussion of scripture.

To contemplate this scripture is not to summon or grasp its imagery and efficacies, nor to visualize its dharmas as though beholding the Jade Emperor—though one should revere the Dao and ponder the True, never daring to treat it lightly. Merely reciting the scripture with the mouth while silently preserving this mind, delving deeply and exploring its profound principles, embodies the scripture within body and heart. This is what is meant by "contemplating the scripture," until unawares, one finds oneself enveloped in clouds of three colors. These clouds arise from the qi of Heaven and Earth. When the contemplation of the scripture reaches great samadhi, uniting with the heart of Heaven, each thought pure and virtuous, auspicious clouds are born, and one becomes unknowingly surrounded by clouds of blue, yellow, and white. With the heart and body serene and upright, the Dragon and Tiger coil and spiral, hence the appearance of these three-colored clouds. If one’s cultivation of the True reaches completion, five-colored clouds will gather, and ninefold radiance will suffuse—this is yet another level. Suddenly, one sees their body radiant with brilliance, ascending to the Heavenly Palace, where hosts of perfected beings descend to welcome them. *E* signifies a brief moment. Suddenly, one’s bodily light blazes forth, resplendent and glorious. (The remainder of this explanation appears earlier.) If the heart harbors a request, ascending to the Heavenly Palace with a petition, all wondrous treasures and divine elixirs will be offered as desired. All the sublime treasures of the Primordial Jade Emperor will be presented at will, naturally obtained. One should not speak of seeking treasures but of receiving them for oneself. It is fitting to follow the prescribed teachings. With a profound and devoted mind, proceed as explained before. The scripture is the true essence of human nature; silent realization and perpetual adherence suffice. Yet, in terms of contemplation, this may seem a secondary meaning. The Confucian texts say: "The indistinct and unseen are depicted, the formless is vividly beheld." They also say: "Vast and boundless, as though above one." Is this, too, a secondary meaning? Alas, those of superior wisdom are few, while the mediocre are many. Without the practice of contemplation, how can one seek the unheard and unseen, fully unite with Heaven, and transcend the ordinary to enter sagehood? Let the scholar reflect on this.

**Chapter Thirty-Two of the Verification Section Continued**
This scripture is such that all Golden Immortals of the Three Times and Great Sages of the Ten Directions have, by following its teachings in cultivation and devotion, transcended to realize the supreme and wondrous Dao. Here, the Jade Emperor is the Teacher of all Heavens and the King of all Sages. This scripture is not only for those who uphold and recite it but must be followed according to its teachings—even the Golden Immortals of the Three Times (past, present, and future). *Golden Immortals* refers to those whose golden elixir is indestructible, the True Immortals of the Great Mystery. The Great Sages of the Ten Directions—the four cardinal, four intermediate, upper, and lower directions—all the revered deities of the Lingbao tradition, the peerless and boundless Great Sages, have cultivated and devoted themselves to this scripture according to its teachings, as explained before. Thus, they transcend to realize the supreme and wondrous Dao. Differences in cultivation lead to varying degrees of attainment. Until one reaches the stage of Dao verification, it is not the supreme. Beings such as spirit immortals, human immortals, earth immortals, ghost immortals, as well as gods of the Desire, Form, and Formless Realms, bodhisattvas, śrāvakas, arhats, and buddhas, along with Confucian exemplars like transformed sages, great sages, virtuous ones, faithful scholars, and worthies—none of these are the ultimate. Only Heavenly Immortals, the Buddha of Scriptures, and the unknowable Sages surpass them; among gods and Confucians, these are the highest. The Golden Immortals and Great Sages, by single-mindedly following this scripture’s teachings, unite with the True Emperor and thus attain the highest immortality, transcending to realize the supreme, true, and wondrous Dao, free from the karmic fruits of the Heavens’ blessings. Here, the Jade Emperor is the Teacher of all Heavens—the thirty-three heavens, thirty-six heavens, twelve thousand heavens, and the three thousand great worlds—all who realize the great Dao through the *August Scripture*. The Jade Emperor is the Teacher of all Heavens, the transformer who perfects the hosts of perfected beings. He is the King of all Sages, for all immortals, Confucian sages, buddhas, gods of the heavens, and all holy beings yield to his virtue, defer to his merits, and acknowledge his supremacy, revering him through endless kalpas. Alas, when the Heavenly Lord within a person is at peace, the Dao may be attained. If one does not cultivate the heart to awaken and unite virtue with Heaven, vainly revering the Supreme Emperor, it is a grievous error. Beware and reflect on this.

**Chapter Thirty-Three of the Verification Section Continued**
Therefore, when an ordinary person encounters this scripture and cultivates it with single-minded devotion, they may even command the Five Emperors as their assistants, summon and employ the hosts of immortals, direct and govern the spirit officials, and guide the workings of yin and yang. A thousand spirits will revere them, ten thousand gods will submit in awe, all evil will avoid their path, and the myriad demons will constrain their forms. After the end of their life, they will immediately undergo refinement in the Southern Palace, ascend to the Upper Clarity, and roam freely and at ease, abiding with the Dao forever. It is not only the Golden Immortals and great worthies who realize the Dao through this scripture. Therefore, when an ordinary person encounters this scripture—an ordinary being of the world who, by karmic conditions, meets this scripture and cultivates it with single-minded devotion—even the Five Emperors (the Five Elder Sovereigns, not the Five Sacred Peaks) will assist their progress and support their shortcomings. They may summon and employ the hosts of immortals, directing and commanding the spirit officials. *Summon and employ* means to issue commands, and the immortals will obey; *direct and command* means to control and dispatch, and the spirit officials will serve. As the scripture says: "Those who cultivate the Dao may command ghosts and drive away spirits." Though an ordinary person, through single-minded devotion to the scripture, the power of the Dao subdues them, enabling them to summon immortals and command spirits, guiding the workings of yin and yang. Only with utmost sincerity and singular focus, faithfully upholding this scripture, can one naturally govern creation, harmonizing yin and yang. A thousand spirits will revere them, a thousand spirits will honor and look up to them. Ten thousand gods will submit in awe—*submit* meaning to be struck with reverent fear and obedience; *awe* meaning to restrain their spirits and submit silently. The ten thousand gods submit without need for coercion. All evil will avoid their path, and the myriad demons will constrain their forms, daring not to appear.

Upon the end of one’s allotted span, the liberation shall be as foretold. They shall attain refinement in the Southern Palace—the Southern Palace being the Vermilion Mound Prefecture, the palace for refining and delivering souls. *Refinement* here denotes the process of being tempered and delivered in the Southern Palace. They shall ascend to the Upper Clarity by flying steps, their spirit soaring to the celestial realms of Upper Clarity—though this is not the true Upper Clarity realm, but merely an expression of ascension to heaven. They shall roam freely and at ease, spared the trials of hell, exempt from karmic retribution, unbound and unshackled, carefree and unfettered. They shall abide with the Dao in perpetuity. From the moment they first uphold this scripture, though not yet perfected, through single-minded devotion and reverent practice, their heart resonates with the Dao, unobstructed and untethered. Their hun-soul and spirit purified, they shall no longer suffer dissolution or descent, but endure with the Dao forever. This is what the Daoist canon means by "death yet not perishing," and what the Buddhist scriptures call "awakening and thus liberation." If even an ordinary person, through single-minded devotion to this scripture, may attain such efficacy, then the power of utmost sincerity is evident. Though of humble intellect, one may yet fulfill the Dao—let the reader take heed.

**Chapter Thirty-Four: Manifest Verifications**

Moreover, when sentient beings in the world hear this scripture and their hearts arise in longing and reverence, they may choose a pure and secluded place within their homes to enshrine the image of the August Lord. Day and night, they shall offer devout worship, morning incense rising in abundance, with fragrant flowers, lamps, fruits, and solemn offerings. They shall honor and venerate, chant the sacred names, gaze in reverence, and recite the scripture. Such a person shall attain thirty sublime virtues:

First, the praise and esteem of all immortals;
Second, the rebirth of departed ancestors in heaven;
Third, the dissolution of past misfortunes;
Fourth, unimpeded passage wherever they go;

**Fifth,** freedom from theft and banditry;
**Sixth,** the fulfillment of all desires;
